Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
10 min
Unavoidable vs Avoidable: What Enters the Throat During Fasting
Expands the matn's ruling on unavoidable vs avoidable substances

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish which substances break the fast upon reaching the throat based on the criterion of unavoidability, and state the Hanafi mufta bihi position regarding tears and sweat.

Core Idea
What enters the throat unavoidably does not break the fast; what is avoidable does.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq expands on the matn's ruling about dust, flies, and tears entering the throat. The commentator explains the legal criterion: avoidability. He also critiques alternative opinions, clarifying the preferred view. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the rules of fasting in daily life.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
List the substances mentioned: dust, fly, smoke (unavoidable) vs tears, sweat, nose blood, rain, snow (avoidable). Note that the ruling hinges on whether the substance could have been prevented by closing the mouth.
The key is that a person can close their mouth to prevent tears/rain, but cannot avoid dust or a fly entering.
2
Understand the rationale for unavoidability
Dust and fly cannot be avoided even with the mouth closed, so they resemble smoke that enters through the nose. The fast remains valid because there is no deliberate action.
The ruling applies when the person is mindful (dhakir) of the fast.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some scholars (al-Khazanah) tie the breaking of the fast to finding saltiness in more than two drops. Others (Fatawa Qadikhan) consider only the actual reaching of the throat. The commentator prefers the latter.
The saltiness criterion is rejected because even a single drop has saltiness, making it unreliable.
4
Note the mufta bihi ruling
For tears and similar avoidable substances, if they reach the throat, the fast is broken (qada required without kaffarah unless the act was done with enjoyment, then kaffarah is added).
Deliberate enjoyment of swallowing tears triggers both qada and kaffarah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (أو دخل حلقه غبار أو ذباب وهو ذاكر لصومه يعني لا يفطر لأن الذباب لا يستطاع الامتناع عنه فشابه الدخان والغبار لدخولهما من الأنف إذا طبق الفم. قيد بما ذكر لأنه لو وصل لحلقه دموعه أو عرق رقه أو دم رعافه أو مطر أو ثلج فسد صومه لتيسر طبق الفم وفتحه أحياناً الاحتراز عن الدخول، وإن ابتلعه متعمداً لزمته الكفارة. واعتبار مع الوصول إلى الحلق في الدمع ونحوه مذكور في فتاوى قاضيخان وهو أولى مما في الخزانة من تقييد الفساد بوجدان الملوحة في الأكثر من قطرتين ونفي الفساد في القطرة والقطرتين لأن الفطرة يجد ملوحتها فلا معول عليه والتعليل في المطر بما ذكرنا أولى مما في الهداية والتبيين من التعليل بإمكان أن تأويه خيمة أو سقف فإنه يقتضي أن المسافر الذي لا يجد ما يأويه ليس حكمه كغيره وليس كذلك. وفي الفتاوى الظهيرية وإذا نزل الدموع من عينيه إلى فمه فابتلعها يجب القضاء بلا كفارة. وفي متفرقات الفقيه أبي جعفر: إن تلذذ بابتلاع الدموع يجب القضاء مع الكفارة وغبار الطاحونة كالدخان قوله: (لأن القطرة يجد ملوحتها كذا في الفتح ثم قال : فالأولى عندي الاعتبار بوجود أن الملوحة لصحيح الحس لأنه لا ضرورة في أكثر من ذلك، وما في فتاوى قاضيخان «لو دخل دمعه أو عرق جبينه أو رعافه حلقه فسد صومه يوافق ما ذكرناه فإنه علق بوصوله إلى الحلق ومجرد وجدان الملوحة دليل ذلك اهـ . قال في النهر : وأقول في الخلاصة في القطرة والقطرتين لا فطر، أما في الأكثر فإن وجد الملوحة في جميع الفم واجتمع شيء كثير وابتلعه أفطر وإلا فلا، وهذا ظاهر في دم
His statement: 'Or dust or a fly enters his throat while he is mindful of his fast' means he does not break his fast because a fly cannot be avoided, so it resembles smoke and dust which enter from the nose when the mouth is closed. He restricted it with what was mentioned because if his tears, or the sweat of his neck, or blood from his nose, or rain, or snow reach his throat, his fast is broken due to the ease of closing the mouth and opening it sometimes to avoid entry. And if he deliberately swallows it, expiation is obligatory upon him. Considering the reaching of the throat in tears and similar is mentioned in Fatawa Qadikhan, and it is more appropriate than what is in al-Khazanah of restricting breaking to finding saltiness in more than two drops and negating breaking in one or two drops, because the nature finds its saltiness, so there is no reliance on it. And the explanation for rain with what we mentioned is more appropriate than what is in al-Hidayah and al-Tabyin of explaining by the possibility of sheltering in a tent or roof, because it implies that a traveler who does not find shelter is not like others, and it is not so. In al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah: If tears descend from his eyes to his mouth and he swallows them, qada is obligatory without expiation. In Mufarriqat al-Faqih Abi Ja'far: If he enjoys swallowing tears, qada with expiation is obligatory. And dust of a mill is like smoke. His statement: 'Because the droplet finds its saltiness, thus in al-Fath then he said: In my opinion, the criterion is the existence of saltiness for one with sound senses because there is no necessity in more than that. And what is in Fatawa Qadikhan: If his tears or the sweat of his forehead or his nosebleed enters his throat, his fast is broken, agrees with what we mentioned, because he tied it to reaching the throat, and mere finding of saltiness is evidence of that.' He said in al-Nahr: I say, in al-Khulasah: in one or two drops, there is no breaking; but in more, if he finds saltiness in the entire mouth and a lot gathers and he swallows it, then he breaks, otherwise no. And this is apparent in blood.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn's ruling that dust or a fly entering the throat does not break the fast. The commentator explains the reasoning: these things cannot be avoided, so they are like smoke. He contrasts them with tears, sweat, nasal blood, rain, and snow, which can be avoided by closing the mouth, so they break the fast. The discussion includes a critique of other opinions: some tie the ruling to finding saltiness in the mouth, but the preferred view (from Fatawa Qadikhan) is to consider actual reaching of the throat. The commentator also notes a difference in ruling for tears: generally, qada without expiation is required, but if one enjoys swallowing tears, expiation is added. The final note from al-Nahr clarifies that a small amount (one or two drops) does not break the fast, but a larger amount that is tasted in the whole mouth does.

Key Terms
غبار (ghubar): Dust; considered unavoidable when entering the throat during fast.
ذباب (dhubab): Fly; similar to dust in being unavoidable.
دموع (dumu'): Tears; if they reach the throat, they break the fast because one can prevent them.
ملوحة (mulūḥah): Saltiness; used in some opinions as a sign that a significant amount has entered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following does NOT break the fast?
A) Tears
B) Sweat from the neck
C) Smoke from incense
D) Rain

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between unavoidable substances (dust, fly, smoke) which do not break the fast, and avoidable substances (tears, rain, etc.) which do break when they reach the throat.

Rulings on Blood, Mucus, and Saliva of Others during Fasting
Expansion of Kanz al-Daqa'iq on nullifiers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criterion of predominance (ghalabah) to determine if blood from teeth or mucus breaks the fast, and state the ruling for swallowing another's saliva.

Core Idea
The fast is broken only when a foreign substance predominates over saliva or is deliberately swallowed from the hand.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq provides detailed rulings for three specific cases a fasting person may face: blood from teeth, nasal mucus, and another person's saliva. The key concepts are predominance (ghalabah) for mixtures, and the origin and intentionality for mucus and saliva. Understanding these details helps avoid unnecessary nullification or overlooking actual breakage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the substance and its source
Determine whether the substance is blood from teeth, nasal mucus, or another person's saliva. Each has a different ruling based on its origin and how it enters the throat.
The ruling for blood from teeth depends on mixing with saliva.
2
Apply the predominance (ghalabah) criterion for blood
If blood from teeth is mixed with saliva, compare their amounts. If saliva predominates, the fast is not broken. If blood predominates or they are equal (as precaution), the fast is broken.
Ghalabah is a standard Hanafi principle for determining the ruling on mixed substances.
3
Analyze the ruling for nasal mucus
If mucus originates from the head and enters the throat directly (from the nose), it is treated like one's own saliva and does not break the fast. Only if the mucus is placed on the palm and then deliberately swallowed does it break the fast.
The key distinction is whether the mucus was deliberately collected and swallowed.
4
Evaluate the ruling for another person's saliva
Swallowing another person's saliva (e.g., from kissing or sharing food) breaks the fast. In the default case, only qada (make-up) is required, not kaffarah (expiation). However, later sources mention that if the saliva is from a friend, kaffarah may be required.
This is an intra-Hanafi difference; the default mufta bihi is no kaffarah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الولوالجية : الدم إذا خرج الأسنان ودخل الحلق إن كانت الغلبة للبزاق لا يفسد صومه، وإن كانت للدم فسد، وكذا إن استويا احتياطاً ثم قال الصائم إذا دخل المخاط أنفه من رأسه ثم استشمه ودخل حلقه على تعمد منه لا شيء عليه لأنه بمنزلة ريقه إلا أن يجعله على كفه ثم يبتلعه فيكون عليه القضاء. وفي الظهيرية وكذا المخاط والبزاق يخرج من فيه أو أنفه فاستشمه واستنشقه وفي فتح القدير: لو ابتلع ريق غيره أفطر ولا كفارة عليه، وليس على من لا يفسد صومه. إطلاقة فسيأتي في آخر الكتاب في مسائل شتى أنه لو ابتلع بزاق غيره كفر لو صديقه وإلا لا وأقره عليه الشارح الزيلعي .
In al-Walwalijiyyah: If blood comes out of the teeth and enters the throat: if the predominance is for saliva, it does not break his fast; if it is for blood, it breaks; similarly, if they are equal, as a precaution. Then he said: The fasting person, if mucus enters his nose from his head, then he sniffs it and it enters his throat deliberately, there is nothing upon him because it is like his saliva, unless he puts it on his palm and then swallows it, then qada is upon him. In al-Zahiriyyah: Similarly, mucus and saliva come out of his mouth or nose, then he sniffs or inhales it. And in Fath al-Qadir: If he swallows the saliva of another, he breaks his fast but no expiation is upon him. It is not upon the one who does not break his fast. It is absolute; it will come at the end of the book in miscellaneous issues that if he swallows the saliva of another, he expiates if it is his friend, otherwise no, and al-Sharih al-Zayla'i affirmed it.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage gives specific rulings for blood from teeth, mucus, and the saliva of another person. For blood from teeth mixed with saliva, the ruling depends on which is predominant. If saliva is more, it does not break the fast; if blood is more, it does; if equal, precaution dictates that it breaks. Mucus that originates from the head and enters the nose and then the throat is like one's own saliva and does not break the fast, unless one deliberately places it on the hand and swallows it. Swallowing another person's saliva breaks the fast but does not require expiation, though there is a later detail that if it is from a friend, expiation may be required. The passage draws on several Hanafi sources including al-Walwalijiyyah, al-Zahiriyyah, and Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
الغلبة (al-ghalabah): Predominance; used to determine whether blood or saliva is more in the mixture.
المخاط (al-mukhat): Nasal mucus; when it originates from the head and enters the throat, it is treated like saliva.
ريق غيره (reeq ghayrihi): Saliva of another person; swallowing it breaks the fast but without expiation in the default case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if mucus enters the fasting person's throat directly from his nose without placing it on his palm?
A) The fast is broken and qada and kaffarah are required
B) The fast is broken and only qada is required
C) The fast is not broken because it is like his own saliva
D) The fast is broken only if he enjoys it

Key Takeaway
The nullification of fast due to swallowed substances depends on predominance, origin, and intentionality; the default ruling for another's saliva is qada without kaffarah.

Ruling on Eating Food Particles Between Teeth and Swallowing Grains
Details the ruling for leftover food particles and small seeds or grains

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on swallowing food particles between teeth and small grains, and apply the chickpea size criterion to determine whether the fast is broken and what penalty applies.

Core Idea
Small unavoidable food between teeth does not break the fast; a chickpea-sized amount does.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies a common scenario for fasting individuals: food trapped between teeth. The text distinguishes negligible amounts from significant quantities, drawing on the authority of Abu Yusuf, al-Dabusi, and later Hanafi masters. It also extends the ruling to seeds and grains like sesame and grapes, providing a framework for determining when swallowing breaks the fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
The act is eating food particles left between teeth. The default ruling is that it does not break the fast if it is a small amount that cannot be avoided, analogous to saliva.
The author did not initially qualify it as small, but later scholars specified.
2
Locate the measure (qadr)
The passage gives several measures: the size of a chickpea (al-Sadr al-Shahid) or what can be swallowed without extra saliva (al-Dabusi). Less than that is considered small.
The chickpea is the standard benchmark in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf holds that a large amount (chickpea size) breaks with qada only, while Zufar argues it does not break at all because a large amount cannot remain between teeth. The chosen view is that it breaks with qada and kaffarah if deliberately swallowed after removal.
The ikhtilaf is on the nature of the penalty, not on whether the fast is broken.
4
Apply the measure to examples
The passage applies the criteria to specific cases: a single sesame seed swallowed whole does not break; a handful does. For a grape, the presence of the 'tafruq' (sprout) affects the ruling on kaffarah.
Chewing small items does not break unless taste is found in the throat.
5
Note the cross-application to prayer
The passage concludes that the same quantity (size of a chickpea) that breaks the fast also invalidates the prayer.
This shows the consistency of the Hanafi framework across acts of worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (أو أكل ما بين أسنانه أي لا يفطر لأنه قليل لا يمكن الاحتراز عنه فجعل بمنزلة الريق، ولم يقيده المصنف بالقلة مع أن الكثير مفسد موجب للقضاء دون الكفارة عند أبي يوسف خلافاً لزفر لما أن الكثير لا يبقى بين الأسنان، وهو مقدار الحمصة على رأي الصدر الشهيد أو ما يمكن أن يبتلعه من غير ريق على ما اختاره الدبوسي واستحسنه ابن الهمام وما دونه قليل وأطلقه فشمل ما إذا ابتلعه أو مضغه وسواء قصد ابتلاعه أو لا كما في غاية البيان. وقيد بأكله لأنه لو أخرجه ثم ابتلعه فسد صومه كما لو ابتلع سمسمة أو حبة من خارج لكن تكلموا في وجوب الكفارة والمختار الوجوب كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان . وهو الصحيح كذا في المحيط بخلاف ما لو مضغها حيث لا يفسد لأنها تتلاشى إلا إذا كان قدر الحمصة فإن صومه يفسد. وفي الكافي في السمسمة قال : إن مضغها لا يفسد إلا إن وجد طعمها في حلقه. قال في فتح القدير : وهذا حسن جداً فليكن الأصل في كل قليل مضغه. وصرح في المحيط بما في الكافي. وفي الفتاوى الظهيرية : روي عن محمد أنه . خرج على أصحابه يوماً وسألهم . عن هذه المسألة فقال : ماذا تقولون في صائم رمضان إذا ابتلع سمسمة واحدة كما هي أيفطر ؟ قالوا : لا. قال : أرأيتم لو أكل كفاً واحدة وابتلع كما هي ؟ قالوا : نعم وعليه الكفارة قال : بالأولى أم بالأخيرة؟ قالوا: لا بل بالأولى. قال الحاكم الإمام محمد بن يوسف : فعلى قياس هذه الرواية يجب القضاء مع اهـ. وتقدم أن وجوب الكفارة هو المختار وذكر قبلها : وإذا ابتلع حبة العنب إن مضغها قضى وكفر، وإن ابتلعها كما هي إن لم يكن معها تفروقها فعليه القضاء والكفارة بالاتفاق، وإن كان معها تفروقها قال عامة العلماء : عليه القضاء مع الكفارة. وقال أبو سهيل : لا كفارة عليه وهو الصحيح لأنها لا تؤكل مع ذلك عادة. وأراد بالتفروق ههنا ما يلتزق بالعنقود من حب العنب وثقبته مسدودة .به وإن ابتلع تفاحة روى هشام عن محمد أن عليه الكفارة ثم ما يفسد الصوم فإنه يفسد الصلاة وهو قدر الحمصة. وفي البزازية : أكل
His statement: 'Or he ate what is between his teeth' meaning he does not break his fast because it is a small amount that cannot be avoided, so it is treated like saliva. The author did not restrict it to a small amount, even though a large amount spoils and necessitates qada without kaffarah according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Zufar, because a large amount does not remain between the teeth. It is the size of a chickpea according to al-Sadr al-Shahid, or what can be swallowed without saliva according to what al-Dabusi chose and Ibn al-Humam approved, and less than that is small. And it is absolute, so it includes whether he swallows it or chews it, and whether he intends to swallow it or not, as in Ghayat al-Bayan. And he restricted it to eating because if he takes it out and then swallows it, his fast is broken, just as if he swallowed a sesame seed or a grain from outside, but they discussed the obligation of kaffarah, and the chosen view is obligation, as in Fatawa Qadikhan. And it is correct as in al-Muhit, contrary to if he chews it, where it does not break because it dissolves, unless it is the size of a chickpea, then his fast breaks. In al-Kafi regarding the sesame seed: he said: if he chews it, it does not break unless he finds its taste in his throat. He said in Fath al-Qadir: This is very good, so let the principle be for every small thing that is chewed. And he stated in al-Muhit what is in al-Kafi. In al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah: It is narrated from Muhammad that he came out to his companions one day and asked them about this issue: 'What do you say about a fasting person in Ramadan if he swallows one sesame seed as it is, does he break his fast?' They said: 'No.' He said: 'Do you see if he ate a handful and swallowed it as is?' They said: 'Yes, and expiation is upon him.' He said: 'By the first or the last?' They said: 'No, rather by the first.' Al-Hakim Imam Muhammad ibn Yusuf said: 'According to the analogy of this narration, qada is obligatory along with...' And it has preceded that the obligation of kaffarah is the chosen view. And before it is mentioned: if he swallows a grape, if he chews it, he performs qada and expiation; if he swallows it as it is, if there is no 'tafruq' with it, then qada and kaffarah are upon him by agreement; if there is 'tafruq' with it, the general body of scholars say qada with kaffarah is upon him; Abu Suhayl said: no kaffarah upon him, and it is correct because it is not normally eaten that way. And he meant by 'tafruq' here what sticks to the bunch of grapes with its hole blocked. And if he swallows an apple, Hisham narrated from Muhammad that kaffarah is upon him. Then that which spoils the fast also spoils the prayer, and it is the size of a chickpea. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: eating...
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage addresses eating food lodged between the teeth while fasting. The general ruling is that if it is a small amount that cannot be avoided, it does not break the fast, similar to saliva. The commentator clarifies that the author did not qualify it as small, but Abu Yusuf holds that a large amount (size of a chickpea) breaks the fast and requires qada without kaffarah, while Zufar disagrees. The preferred view is that the defining amount is what can be swallowed without added saliva, as per al-Dabusi. If one removes the food and then swallows it, the fast is broken, and expiation is required, according to Fatawa Qadikhan. Chewing the food until it dissolves is treated differently: it does not break the fast unless the chewed amount is the size of a chickpea. The narration from Imam Muhammad about a sesame seed illustrates that swallowing a single seed whole does not break the fast, but a handful does. For a grape, the ruling depends on whether it is swallowed with its 'tafruq' (the sprout that seals the hole); if without, all agree it breaks fast with qada and kaffarah; if with, there is a difference: Abu Suhayl says no kaffarah because it is not normally eaten that way. Finally, the passage notes that the quantity that breaks the fast (chickpea size) also invalidates prayer.

Key Terms
ما بين الأسنان (ma bayna al-asnan): Food particles lodged between teeth; if small, they do not break the fast.
الحمصة (al-himmasah): A chickpea; used as a measure for the amount that breaks the fast.
السمسمة (al-simsimah): A single sesame seed; swallowing it whole does not break the fast.
التفروق (al-tafruq): The sprout that sticks to a grape from the bunch, sealing its hole.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The fast is not broken unless he finds its taste in his throat
D) The fast is broken only if it is the size of a chickpea

Key Takeaway
Small, unavoidable food between teeth does not break the fast; the chickpea size is the benchmark for determining when it does, with qada and kaffarah required for deliberate acts.

Ruling on Eating Food Remaining Between Teeth: The Principle of Unavoidability
Expansion on ruling that small residues between teeth do not break the fast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on swallowing food residues between teeth during fasting and distinguish between small unavoidable amounts (excused) and large avoidable amounts (breaking fast), including the intra-Hanafi differences on kaffarah.

Core Idea
Small residues between teeth are like saliva; large ones break the fast but may not require expiation.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the ruling that eating what is between the teeth does not break the fast. The commentator explains the rationale: small amounts that cannot be guarded against are treated like saliva. However, the ruling changes when the amount is significant—defined as the size of a chickpea or what can be swallowed without saliva. The passage also explores related cases like swallowing sesame seeds, grapes with stems, and apples, highlighting nuanced differences among Hanafi authorities on whether kaffarah (expiation) is obligatory.

Guided Path
1
State the default ruling and its illa
Identify the act (eating residues between teeth) and the default ruling: it does not break the fast. Then locate the legal cause (illa): it is a small amount that cannot be guarded against, so it is treated like saliva (analogy).
This is the foundational principle: unavoidability excuses.
2
Define the threshold for qaleel and kathir
The passage gives two definitions for 'much' (kathir): the size of a chickpea (hamsa) or what can be swallowed without saliva. Any less is 'little' (qaleel). Note that kathir breaks the fast while qaleel does not.
Al-Dabusi and Ibn al-Humam both weigh in on the definition.
3
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the effect of kathir
Abu Yusuf holds that swallowing a large amount between teeth breaks the fast but requires only qada (no kaffarah). Zufar holds it does not break the fast at all because such large amounts do not remain between teeth. The mufta bihi is Abu Yusuf's view.
This disagreement is on the consequence, not the principle of unavoidability.
4
Analyze the extension to external items (sesame, grape, apple)
The passage contrasts internal residues with external items. For example, swallowing a whole sesame seed: if it is one seed, it does not break the fast; a handful does, with kaffarah. For a grape with its stem (tafruq), the majority require kaffarah, but Abu Sahl exempts it because the stem is not normally eaten.
The key factor is whether the item is normally consumed and whether it is avoidable.
5
Summarize the general rule for eating during fasting
The passage concludes with the principle that whatever spoils the fast also spoils the prayer, and the threshold is the size of a chickpea (hamsa). This is a consistent benchmark for mufsidat.
This general rule connects fasting and prayer rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله (أو أكل ما بين (أسنانه أي لا يفطر لأنه قليل لا يمكن الاحتراز عنه فجعل بمنزلة الريق، ولم يقيده المصنف بالقلة مع أن الكثير مفسد موجب للقضاء دون الكفارة عند أبي يوسف خلافاً لزفر لما أن الكثير لا يبقى بين الأسنان، وهو مقدار الحمصة على رأي الصدر الشهيد أو ما يمكن أن يبتلعه من غير ريق على ما اختاره الدبوسي واستحسنه ابن الهمام وما دونه قليل وأطلقه فشمل ما إذا ابتلعه أو مضغه وسواء قصد ابتلاعه أو لا كما في غاية البيان. وقيد بأكله لأنه لو أخرجه ثم ابتلعه فسد صومه كما لو ابتلع سمسمة أو حبة من خارج لكن تكلموا في وجوب الكفارة والمختار الوجوب كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان . وهو الصحيح كذا في المحيط بخلاف ما لو مضغها حيث لا يفسد لأنها تتلاشى إلا إذا كان قدر الحمصة فإن صومه يفسد. وفي الكافي في السمسمة قال : إن مضغها لا يفسد إلا إن وجد طعمها في حلقه. قال في فتح القدير : وهذا حسن جداً فليكن الأصل في كل قليل مضغه. وصرح في المحيط بما في الكافي. وفي الفتاوى الظهيرية : روي عن محمد أنه . خرج على أصحابه يوماً وسألهم . عن هذه المسألة فقال : ماذا تقولون في صائم رمضان إذا ابتلع سمسمة واحدة كما هي أيفطر ؟ قالوا : لا. قال : أرأيتم لو أكل كفاً واحدة وابتلع كما هي ؟ قالوا : نعم وعليه الكفارة قال : بالأولى أم بالأخيرة؟ قالوا: لا بل بالأولى. قال الحاكم الإمام محمد بن يوسف : فعلى قياس هذه الرواية يجب القضاء مع اهـ. وتقدم أن وجوب الكفارة هو المختار وذكر قبلها : وإذا ابتلع حبة العنب إن مضغها قضى وكفر، وإن ابتلعها كما هي إن لم يكن معها تفروقها فعليه القضاء والكفارة بالاتفاق، وإن كان معها تفروقها قال عامة العلماء : عليه القضاء مع الكفارة. وقال أبو سهيل : لا كفارة عليه وهو الصحيح لأنها لا تؤكل مع ذلك عادة. وأراد بالتفروق ههنا ما يلتزق بالعنقود من حب العنب وثقبته مسدودة .به وإن ابتلع تفاحة روى هشام عن محمد أن عليه الكفارة ثم ما يفسد الصوم فإنه يفسد الصلاة وهو قدر الحمصة.
His saying (or ate what is between his teeth): meaning he does not break fast because it is little that cannot be guarded against, so it is made like saliva. And the author did not restrict it to littleness while much spoils and necessitates qada without kaffarah according to Abu Yusuf contrary to Zufar because much does not remain between the teeth, and it is the amount of a chickpea according to the opinion of al-Sadr al-Shahid or what can be swallowed without saliva according to what al-Dabusi chose and Ibn al-Humam deemed good, and less than that is little. And in al-Kafi concerning the sesame seed: he said: if he chews it it does not spoil unless he finds its taste in his throat. He said in Fath al-Qadir: this is very good, so let the principle in every little thing that is chewed be this. And it is clearly stated in al-Muhit what is in al-Kafi. And in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya: it is narrated from Muhammad that he came out to his companions one day and asked them about this issue and said: what do you say about a fasting person in Ramadan if he swallows one sesame seed as it is, does he break fast? They said: no. He said: do you see if he ate a handful and swallowed it as it is? They said: yes and upon him is kaffarah. He said: by the first or the last? They said: no, rather by the first. Al-Hakim Imam Muhammad ibn Yusuf said: by analogy of this narration, qada is obligatory with it. And it preceded that the obligation of kaffarah is the chosen view. And he mentioned before it: and if he swallows a grape if he chews it he does qada and kaffarah, and if he swallows it as it is if there is not with it its tafruq then upon him is qada and kaffarah by agreement, and if there is with it its tafruq the majority of scholars say: upon him is qada with kaffarah. And Abu Sahl said: no kaffarah upon him and it is correct because it is not usually eaten with that. And he intended by tafruq here what attaches to the bunch of the grape and its hole is blocked with it. And if he swallows an apple it is narrated from Hisham from Muhammad that upon him is kaffarah. Then what spoils the fast spoils the prayer, and it is the amount of a chickpea.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expansion on the ruling that eating what is between the teeth does not break the fast. The reason given is that it is a small amount that cannot be avoided, so it is treated like saliva. The commentator notes that the author (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) did not qualify the ruling with 'little', although a large amount would break the fast and require qada (compensatory fast) but not kaffarah (expiation) according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Zufar who reportedly held that a large amount also does not break because it does not remain between the teeth. The definition of 'much' is the size of a chickpea according to al-Sadr al-Shahid, or what can be swallowed without saliva according to al-Dabusi, with less than that being considered little. The ruling applies whether the person swallows the residue or chews it, and whether or not they intended to swallow it. However, if one removes it from the mouth and then swallows it, the fast is broken, similar to swallowing a sesame seed or any external grain. There is a difference of opinion on whether kaffarah is required in such cases; the chosen position is that it is obligatory. If the residue is chewed, it does not break the fast unless it is the size of a chickpea, because it dissolves. Al-Kafi adds that chewing a sesame seed does not break the fast unless its taste reaches the throat. Fath al-Qadir finds this good and extends it to any small item that is chewed. A narration from Imam Muhammad illustrates the principle: swallowing one sesame seed whole does not break the fast, but swallowing a handful does require kaffarah; the kaffarah is for the first handful, not each grain. For a grape: if chewed, qada and kaffarah are required. If swallowed whole without its cluster stem (tafruq), there is consensus on qada and kaffarah. If swallowed with the stem, the majority require qada and kaffarah, but Abu Sahl holds no kaffarah, which is correct because the stem is not normally eaten. The same applies to an apple: a narration from Hisham from Muhammad requires kaffarah. Finally, the general principle is stated: whatever spoils the fast also spoils the prayer, and the threshold is the size of a chickpea.

Key Terms
قليل (qaleel): A small amount of food between teeth, less than the size of a chickpea (hamsa) or less than what can be swallowed without saliva.
كثير (kathir): A large amount of food between teeth, defined as the size of a chickpea (hamsa) or what can be swallowed without saliva.
تفروق (tafruq): The stem or piece of the grape cluster that attaches the grape to the bunch, not normally eaten.
حمصة (hamsa): A chickpea; used as a standard measure for the amount that spoils the fast and prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a fasting person chews a sesame seed but does not taste it in the throat, what is the ruling?
A) The fast is broken and qada and kaffarah are required.
B) The fast is broken and qada is required without kaffarah.
C) The fast is not broken.
D) The fast is not broken unless the seed is the size of a chickpea.

Key Takeaway
Small unavoidable food residues between teeth do not break the fast, but large amounts (chickpea-sized) do, with qada required; external items swallowed whole may require kaffarah depending on amount and custom.

Vomiting and the Fast: Intentionality and the Mouthful Threshold
Hadith-based expansion on vomiting rules with intra-Hanafi disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on vomiting during fasting, distinguish between controlled and uncontrolled vomiting, and apply the mouthful threshold to determine if the fast is broken.

Core Idea
Vomiting breaks the fast only if intentional; the 'mouthful' threshold determines if a return of vomit is considered an exit.

The Hanafi school relies on a prophetic hadith to distinguish between involuntary vomiting (no break) and induced vomiting (break). However, what about when vomit returns? The scholars debated whether the amount matters and whether intention to break is required. This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and establishes the mufta bihi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling from the hadith
The hadith states: uncontrolled vomiting (dhara'ahu al-qay') does not break the fast nor require qada; induced vomiting (istiqa') requires qada. This is the foundational ruling.
The hadith is cited from the Sunan collections (Abu Dawud, Tirmidhi, etc.).
2
Understand the term 'returned' (al-'awd)
The matn adds 'or returned' to clarify that mere vomiting without returning does not break the fast a fortiori. The return of vomit, whether intentional or not, is the subject of detailed discussion.
The phrase 'or returned' in the matn expands the ruling to cover cases where vomit re-enters the stomach.
3
Classify the amount of vomit
The amount of vomit matters: less than a mouthful is not considered an 'exit' in shari'a; a mouthful or more is an exit. This threshold influences the rulings for returning vomit.
The Hanafi school defines 'mouthful' as what fills the mouth; it is a measurable benchmark.
4
Map the four scenarios from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj
The text presents four cases based on amount (mouthful or less) and whether the return is intentional or by itself. Each case has a ruling and a dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
These scenarios illustrate the application of the principles of 'exit' and 'deliberate action' (al-sun'ah).
5
Determine the tarjih in each scenario
The correct view (al-sahih) according to the text: Muhammad's view prevails for non-intentional return of a mouthful (no break); Abu Yusuf's view is chosen for inducing vomiting (only mouthful breaks). The mufta bihi integrates both imams' positions.
The tarjih is drawn from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and other later Hanafi works.

His saying (or vomited and returned and did not break fast) due to the hadith of the Sunan: 'Whoever is overcome by vomiting while fasting, there is no qada upon him; and whoever induces vomiting, let him make qada.' And he only mentioned 'returned' to indicate that mere vomiting without returning does not break fast a fortiori. And he mentioned it absolutely so it includes whether it filled the mouth or not, and in the case where it returned and filled the mouth there is dispute with Abu Yusuf, and the correct view is the saying of Muhammad due to the absence of deliberate action and the absence of the form of breaking fast which is swallowing, and likewise its meaning because one does not nourish from it; rather the soul abhors it. His saying (and if he returns it or induces vomiting or swallows a pebble or iron, he only makes qada): meaning he returned the vomit or vomited intentionally and swallowed what is not normally nourished by or used as medicine, so his fast becomes spoiled and qada is incumbent upon him without kaffarah. And he mentioned absolutely in 'returns' so it includes if it did not fill the mouth, and that is the saying of Muhammad due to the presence of deliberate action. And Abu Yusuf said: it does not become spoiled due to the lack of exit in shari'a, and that is the chosen view, so it must be restricted to filling the mouth. And he mentioned absolutely in 'induces vomiting' so it includes if it did not fill the mouth, and that is the saying of Muhammad, and it does not break fast according to Abu Yusuf, and that is the chosen view. But the author mentioned in al-Kafiya that the apparent narration is like the saying of Muhammad. And he did not restrict inducing vomiting to intentional as in al-Hidayah because he preceded that forgetfulness does not break fast; and what is in Ghayat al-Bayan that mentioning 'intentional' is a confirmation because it cannot be except intentional, is rejected, because intentional here means intending to break the fast, not intending to vomit. So the summary is that there are twelve scenarios because it is either overcome by vomiting or induces vomiting, and each one either... He said (the four issues): as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. The first: if it is less than a mouthful and returns or something from it the size of a chickpea, he does not break fast by consensus. According to Abu Yusuf, it is not an exit because it is less than a mouthful; according to Muhammad, there is no deliberate action in entering. The second: if it is a mouthful and he returns it or something from it the size of a chickpea or more, he breaks fast by consensus. According to Abu Yusuf, because it is a mouthful so it was an exit, and whatever exits when he enters his stomach spoils his fast. Muhammad says: deliberate action has occurred from him. The third: if it is less than a mouthful and he returns it or something from it, he breaks fast according to Muhammad for what passed, and according to Abu Yusuf he does not break fast for what passed. The fourth: if it is a mouthful and it returns by itself or something from it the amount of a chickpea or more, he breaks fast according to Abu Yusuf, and according to Muhammad he does not, and it is correct because neither the form of breaking fast (swallowing by his action) nor its meaning (nourishment) exists, and just as one cannot guard against its exit, likewise its return, so it is excused.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling of vomiting and its impact on the fast. It begins by citing the hadith from the Sunan: uncontrolled vomiting does not break the fast nor require qada, but intentional vomiting requires qada. The commentator discusses the use of the word 'returned' in the matn to clarify that mere vomiting without returning the vomit certainly does not break the fast. The absolute wording includes both small and large amounts. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the case where the vomit returns and fills the mouth: Abu Yusuf holds it breaks the fast because it is considered an exit, while Muhammad holds it does not because there is no deliberate swallowing. The correct view is Muhammad's due to lack of intention and lack of nourishment. The matn continues: if one returns vomit intentionally (by swallowing it back) or induces vomiting intentionally, or swallows a pebble or iron piece, the fast is broken but only qada is required, not kaffarah, because these items are not normally eaten or used as medicine. A more detailed analysis follows: the status of vomit as 'exit' depends on whether it fills the mouth. The commentator presents four detailed cases from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: (1) Vomit less than a mouthful returns by itself – no break by consensus. (2) Vomit a mouthful returns by itself – break by consensus. (3) Vomit less than a mouthful intentionally returned – break according to Muhammad, but not according to Abu Yusuf. (4) Vomit a mouthful returns by itself – break according to Abu Yusuf, but not according to Muhammad (which is correct). The underlying principles are: what is less than a mouthful is not considered 'exit' in shari'a, and for intentional return, Muhammad focuses on the presence of deliberate action, while Abu Yusuf focuses on the status of the vomit.

Key Terms
ذرعه القيء (dhara'ahu al-qay'): Vomiting that overcomes a person involuntarily, without any deliberate action
استقاء (istiqa'): Inducing vomiting deliberately
ملء الفم (mil' al-fam): The amount of vomit that fills the mouth, considered a significant 'exit' in shari'a

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between controlled and uncontrolled vomiting; amount (mouthful) and intention are decisive factors, with detailed tarjih between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

When Does Vomiting Break the Fast?
Ruling on vomiting and returning it in fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on vomiting during fasting, and distinguish between the views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the quantity of vomit that breaks the fast.

Core Idea
The fast breaks only if vomiting is intentional or if returned vomit fills the mouth.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on vomiting (qay') during fasting, based on the hadith of the Sunan. The central issue is whether the quantity of vomit matters, especially when it is returned. Within the Hanafi school, there is an important ikhtilaf between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the threshold of mil' al-fam (mouthful). The preferred view is that of Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what exactly is being ruled upon: vomiting (both involuntary and intentional) and returning vomit. The ruling is that involuntary vomiting does not break the fast, but intentional vomiting or returning vomit breaks the fast and requires qada (make-up fast) but not kaffarah.
The hadith differentiates between 'qā'a' (overcome by vomiting) and 'istaqā'a' (intentionally induced vomiting).
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith: 'Whoever is overcome by vomiting while fasting, there is no qada upon him; if he vomits intentionally, let him make it up.' This is a sunnah ahad report from Abu Dawood, Tirmidhi, etc.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut, but the Hanafi school accepts it for establishing rulings.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith explicitly distinguishes between involuntary vomiting (no qada) and intentional vomiting (qada required). The Hanafi jurists deduce that the mere act of vomiting without intention does not break the fast.
The phrase 'istaqā'a' indicates purposeful action, which is key to the ruling.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad holds that any intentional return of vomit, regardless of quantity, breaks the fast because the act of returning (suni') is present. Imam Abu Yusuf holds that only if the returned vomit fills the mouth (mil' al-fam) does it break the fast, because less than that is not legally considered 'exit'.
This ikhtilaf arises from the definition of 'khuruj' (exit) and the presence of intentional action.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The text sides with Abu Yusuf and states 'wa huwa al-mukhtar' (this is the chosen view). Thus the operative fatwa is that only mouthful return breaks the fast.
The tarjih is based on the idea that a small amount of vomit that returns is not equivalent to purposeful ingestion and is excused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم باب ما يفسد الصوم وما لا يفسده
أسنانه أو قاء وعاد لم يفطر وإن أعاده أو استقاء أو ابتلع حصاة أو حديدا قضى فقط

قوله ( وإن أعاده أو استقاء أو ابتلع حصاة أو حديدا قضى فقط) أي أعاد القيء أو قاء عامداً وابتلع ما لا يتغذى به ولا يتداوى به عادة فسد صومه ولزمه القضاء ولا كفارة عليه. وأطلق في الإعادة فشمل ما إذا لم يملأ الفم وهو قول محمد لوجود الصنع. وقال أبو يوسف : لا يفسد لعدم الخروج شرعاً وهو المختار فلا بد من التقييد بملء الفم. وأطلق في الاستقاء فشمل ما إذا لم يملأ الفم وهو قول محمد ولا يفطر عند أبي يوسف وهو المختار لكن ذكر المصنف في كافية أن ظاهر الرواية كقول محمد وإنما لم يقيد الاستقاء بالعمد كما في الهداية لم قدمه أن النسيان لا يفطر، وما في غاية البيان أن ذكر العمد الاستقاء تأكيد لأنه مع لا يكون إلا العمد مردود، لأن العمد يخرج النسيان أي متعمد الفطره لا متعمداً للقيء . فالحاصل أن صور المسائل اثنا عشر لأنه لا يخلو إما إن ذرعه القيء أو استقاء، وكل منهما لا مع الخ) قال الرملي عن القاموس : التفروق بالضم قمع الثمرة أو ما يلتزق به قمعها جمعه تفاريق قوله : لعدم الخروج شرعاً لأن ما دون ملء الفم ليس له حكم الخارج لأنه يمكن ضبطه بخلاف ما كان ملء الفم فإن له حكم الخارج، وفائدته تظهر في أربع مسائل كما في السراج الوهاج : أحدها إذا كان أقل من ملء الفم وعاد أو شيء منه قدر الحمصة لم يفطر إجماعاً، أما عند أبي يوسف فإنه ليس بخارج لأنه أقل من ملء الفم، وعند محمد لا صنع له في الإدخال والثانية إن كان ملء الفم وأعاده أو شيئاً . منه قدر الحمصة فصاعداً أفطر إجماعاً، أما عند أبي يوسف فلأنه ملء الفم فكان خارجاً وما كان خارجاً إذا أدخله جوفه فسد صومه محمد يقول : قد وجد منه الصنع. والثالثة إذا كان أقل من ملء الفم وأعاده أو شيئاً منه أفطر عند محمد لما مر وعند أبي يوسف لا يفطر لما . مر. والرابعة إذا كان ملء الفم وعاد بنفسه أو شيء منه مقدار الحمصة فصاعداً أفطر عند أبي يوسف، وعند محمد لا وهو الصحيح لأنه لو لم يوجد صورة الفطر وهو الابتلاع بصنعه ولا معناه لأنه لا يتغذى به ولأنه كما لا يمكن الاحتراز عن خروجه فكذا عن عوده فجعل عفواً ا هـ.

كتاب الصوم باب ما يفسد الصوم وما لا يفسده
euroscience and that
Book of Fasting, Chapter of What Breaks the Fast and What Does Not Break It. ... He said: 'Or he vomited and it returned, he does not break his fast.' Based on the hadith of the Sunan: 'Whoever is overcome by vomiting while fasting, there is no qada upon him, and if he vomits intentionally, let him make it up.' ... Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling on vomiting (qay') during fasting. The base ruling: if one is overcome by vomiting (without intention) and the vomit returns on its own, the fast is not broken. However, if one intentionally vomits (istaqa'a) or returns the vomit, then the fast is broken and only qada (make-up fast) is required, no kaffarah. The central ikhtilaf between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad concerns whether the amount matters. Abu Yusuf conditions that the vomit must fill the mouth (mil' al-fam) to be considered as causing breaking of the fast, because less than that is not deemed as 'exit' in legal terms. Muhammad holds that any intentional return of vomit breaks the fast, regardless of quantity, because the act of returning is present (suni') even if the amount is small. The preferred view (muhtar) is that of Abu Yusuf: only if it fills the mouth does it break the fast. The passage then enumerates twelve sub-cases based on: (1) involuntary vs intentional vomiting, (2) whether it fills the mouth or not, (3) whether it returns by itself or is returned, and (4) whether it exits or not. The ruling also relates to ablution (wudu') and prayer: vomiting less than mouthful does not break wudu', but more does; similarly for prayer, if less than mouthful, prayer is not invalidated. The differences are analyzed based on the legal analogy (qiyas) of what constitutes 'exit' from the body and the presence of intentional action.

Key Terms
قاء (qā'a): Vomited (involuntarily or intentionally)
استقاء (istaqā'a): Intentionally induced vomiting
ملء الفم (mil' al-fam): The amount that fills the mouth

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view (mufta bihi), when does returning vomit break the fast?
A) Any amount of vomit returned intentionally
B) Only if the vomit fills the mouth (mil' al-fam)
C) Only if the vomit is more than a chickpea (hamsah)
D) Only if the vomit is returned more than once

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on vomiting during fasting hinges on intentionality and, for returned vomit, the quantity: only a mouthful breaks the fast according to the preferred view.

The Ruling on Vomiting and Induced Vomiting in Fasting
Explains the scenarios of vomiting and their effect on fasting, wudu, and prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two exceptions where vomiting breaks the fast in the Hanafi school and explain the reasoning behind the preferred view on phlegm.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, vomiting only breaks the fast if it is a deliberate mouthful — same rule applies to induced phlegm.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks the nuanced rulings on vomiting during fasting. It clarifies the key distinction between a mouthful and less, and between deliberate and involuntary vomiting. The author also addresses a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute about phlegm, explaining why Abu Yusuf's view is preferred for fasting but the majority view is retained for wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and two exceptions
The text states that the fast is not broken in most vomiting scenarios except in two cases: when the vomit is returned to the stomach (i'ada) and when vomiting is induced (istiqa'), both on condition that it fills the mouth.
These are the only situations where the fast is invalidated by vomiting according to the relied-upon opinion.
2
Understand the 'mouthful' threshold
The quantity 'mil' al-fam' (mouthful) is the decisive criterion. Vomit less than that, even if deliberate, does not break the fast. This threshold applies to both natural and induced vomiting.
This is a key distinction from other schools.
3
Distinguish between returning vomit and inducing it
Returning vomit (i'ada) means after it exits, you deliberately swallow it back. Inducing vomiting (istiqa') means you cause yourself to vomit. Both require the mouthful condition.
Unintentional vomiting, even if a mouthful, does not break the fast.
4
Analyze the phlegm dispute
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that induced phlegm does not break the fast, while Abu Yusuf says it does if it fills the mouth. The author prefers Abu Yusuf's view for fasting because the illa (effective cause) is the act of bringing something out, not the substance's nature. However, for wudu, the other view is better.
This shows how the same substance can be treated differently based on the legal context.
5
Apply the ruling to multiple episodes
If a person induces vomiting a mouthful multiple times in one sitting, qada is required. If in separate sessions (morning, noon, evening), no qada. According to Muhammad, the unity of cause (not sitting) determines whether multiple episodes count as one.
This affects the obligation to make up the fast.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يخلو إما أن يملأ الفم أو لا، وكل من الأربعة إما أن عاد بنفسه أو أعاده أو خرج ولم يعده ولا عاد بنفسه وأن صومه لا يفسد على الأصح في الجميع إلا في مسألتين: في الإعادة بشرط ملء الفم، وفي الاستقاء بشرط ملء الفم، وأن وضوءه ينتقض إلا فيما إذا لم يملأ الفم، وأما الصلاة ففي الظهيرية منها : لو قاء أقل من ملء الفم لم تفسد صلاته، وإن أعاده إلى جوفه يجب أن يكون على قياس الصوم عند أبي يوسف لا تفسد، وعن محمد تفسد، وإن تقيأ في صلاته إن كان أقل من ملء الفم لا تفسد صلاته، وإن كان ملء الفم تفسد صلاته اهـ. وفي الخلاصة من فصل الحدث في الصلاة فلو قاء إن كان من غير قصده يبنى إذا لم يتكلم، وإن تقيأ لا يبنى. وهذا إذا كان ملء الفم، فإن كان أقل من ذلك لا تفسد صلاته فلا حاجة إلى البناء .اهـ. وأطلق في أنواع القيء والاستقاء فشمل ما إذا استقاء بلغماً ملء الفم وهو قول أبي يوسف، وعند أبي حنيفة ومحمد لا يفسد صومه بناء على الاختلاف في انتقاض الطهارة، وقول أبي يوسف هنا أحسن، وقولهما في عدم النقض به أحسن لأن الفطر إنما أنيط بما يدخل أو بالقيء عمداً من غير نظر إلى طهارة ونجاسة فلا فرق بين البلغم وغيره بخلاف نقض الطهارة. كذا في فتح القدير وتعبيري بالاستقاء في البلغم أولى مما في الشرح وغيره من التعبير بالقيء كما لا يخفى ولو استقاء مراراً من مجلس ملء فيه لزمه القضاء، إن كان في مجالس أو غدوة ثم نصف النهار ثم عشية لا يلزمه كذا في خزانة الأكمل. وتعبيرى بالاستقاء أولى من التعبير بالقيء كما في الشرح . وينبغي أن يعتبر عند محمد اتحاد السبب لا المجلس كما في نقض الوضوء وأن يكون هو الصحيح كما في النقض، وينبغي أن يكون ما في الخزانة مفرعاً على قول أبي يوسف، أما على قول محمد فإنه يبطل صومه بالمرة الأولى.
It comes to either filling the mouth or not. And each of the four [scenarios] is either it returns by itself or he returns it, or it exits and he does not return it and it does not return by itself, and that his fast is not broken according to the most sound opinion in all except two cases: in returning [it] on condition of filling the mouth, and in inducing vomiting on condition of filling the mouth. And that his ablution is nullified except when he does not fill the mouth. As for prayer, in al-Zahiriyyah from it: if he vomited less than a mouthful, his prayer is not invalidated; and if he returns it to his stomach, it must be on analogy to fasting according to Abu Yusuf: it is not invalidated; and from Muhammad: it is invalidated. And if he vomits deliberately in his prayer, if it is less than a mouthful, his prayer is not invalidated; if it is a mouthful, his prayer is invalidated. End quote. And in al-Khulasah, from the section on hadath in prayer: if he vomited without intention, he builds [upon his prayer] if he did not speak; if he deliberately vomits, he does not build. And this is if it is a mouthful; if it is less than that, his prayer is not invalidated so there is no need to build. End quote. And he made absolute the types of vomiting and induced vomiting, so it includes the case when he induced phlegm filling the mouth, and this is the saying of Abu Yusuf. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, his fast is not broken based on the difference regarding the nullification of purity. The saying of Abu Yusuf here is better, and their saying regarding non-nullification of purity is better, because breaking the fast is linked to that which enters or to deliberate vomiting without regard to purity and impurity, so there is no difference between phlegm and other than it, contrary to the nullification of purity. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. My expression 'by inducing vomiting' regarding phlegm is more appropriate than the expression 'by vomiting' in the Sharh and others, as is not hidden. And if he induced vomiting repeatedly from one sitting with a mouthful each time, qada is incumbent upon him; if it was in different sessions or morning then noon then evening, it is not incumbent. Thus in Khizanat al-Akmal. My expression 'by inducing vomiting' is more appropriate than the expression 'by vomiting' as in Sharh. And it is fitting that according to Muhammad, the unity of cause is considered, not the sitting, as in the nullification of wudu, and that it is the correct view. And it is fitting that what is in Khizanah is derived from the saying of Abu Yusuf. As for according to Muhammad, his fast is invalidated from the first time.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discussing the rulings on vomiting (قيء) and induced vomiting (استقاء) in the context of fasting. The author begins by outlining the possible scenarios: whether the vomit fills the mouth or not, and whether it returns by itself, is returned by the person, or exits and is not returned. The general rule is that the fast is not broken in most cases, except in two specific situations: (1) when the person returns the vomit to his stomach after it has come out, provided it was a mouthful, and (2) when the person intentionally induces vomiting and it is a mouthful. This is according to the most sound opinion (على الأصح). The passage also notes the effect on wudu: it is nullified unless the vomit was less than a mouthful. The author then shifts to prayer (صلاة), quoting from al-Zahiriyyah that vomiting less than a mouthful does not invalidate prayer, but returning it does according to Muhammad, though Abu Yusuf disagrees. The discussion then addresses the case of phlegm (بلغم): Abu Yusuf holds that inducing phlegm that fills the mouth breaks the fast, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say it does not. The author prefers Abu Yusuf's view in this context because breaking the fast is linked to what enters the body or to deliberate vomiting, regardless of purity. However, regarding wudu, the view of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is better. The passage concludes with the ruling on multiple episodes of vomiting: if done in one sitting, qada is required; if in separate sessions, no qada. Finally, the author notes that according to Muhammad, the unity of cause is considered rather than the unity of the sitting, so if the cause is the same, multiple episodes may be treated as one.

Key Terms
القيء (al-qay'): Vomiting: the expulsion of stomach contents through the mouth.
الاستقاء (al-istiqa'): Induced vomiting: deliberately causing oneself to vomit.
ملء الفم (mil' al-fam): Filling the mouth: the quantity of vomit that fills the mouth cavity.
البلغم (al-balgham): Phlegm: a viscous secretion from the respiratory tract, considered by some as equivalent to vomit

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When it is returned to the stomach and when it is induced, provided it fills the mouth
B) When it is less than a mouthful and when it is phlegm
C) When it is intentional and when it is unintentional
D) When it fills the mouth and when it is returned by itself

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires both deliberateness and a mouthful for vomiting to break the fast, and the same rule applies to induced phlegm according to the preferred view.

Swallowing Non-Nutritive Objects: When Kaffara Applies
Explains the ruling on swallowing objects that are not food or medicine, and when kaffara is required.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on swallowing non-nutritive objects while fasting and identify the conditions under which kaffara becomes obligatory.

Core Idea
Swallowing inedible objects breaks the fast but only requires kaffara when it conveys benefit or is done repeatedly in disobedience.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tackles a nuanced area of fasting law: swallowing objects that are neither food nor medicine. While the fast is broken because something enters the stomach, the question of expiation (kaffara) depends on whether the substance provides any benefit to the body. The author also discusses exceptions for repeated disobedience and for cases where the object is chewed or customarily eaten.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is swallowing (al-ibtila') a non-nutritive, non-medicinal substance. The default ruling: the fast becomes fasid (void) requiring qada, but no kaffara is obligatory.
The Hanafi term 'ibtila'' is used deliberately to exclude chewing.
2
Locate the legal cause (illa) for kaffara
Kaffara requires a complete offense (jinaya) which means delivering benefit to the body. Swallowing pebbles lacks this meaning, so kaffara is negated.
This is a qiyas reasoning based on the absence of the effective cause.
3
Note the exception for repeated disobedience
The text quotes al-Qunyah: if someone breaks the fast multiple times with dust or clay deliberately to disobey, kaffara becomes wajib as a deterrent (zajran).
This exception overrides the general rule by istihsan.
4
Analyze the detailed cases: chewed vs. swallowed whole
For nuts and fruits, if chewed they become like food and require kaffara; if swallowed whole, no kaffara. The distinction is whether chewing makes the substance edible.
Examples: moist walnut/almond – no kaffara if swallowed whole; yes if chewed.
5
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad reports that swallowing small watermelon, peach, or myrobalan requires kaffara. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the majority view that kaffara is not required for these.
The author affirms the fatwa of the imams of the cities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا ابتلع ما لا يتغذى به ولا يتداوى به كالحصاة والحديد فلوجود صورة الفطر ولا كفارة لعدم معناه وهو إيصال ما فيه نفع البدن إلى الجوف فقصرت الجناية وهي لا تجب إلا بكمالها فانتفت وفي القنية : أفطر في رمضان مرة بعد أخرى بتراب أو مدر لأجل المعصية فعليه الكفارة زجراً ،له وكتب غيره نعم الفتوى على أفتى أئمة الأمصار وإنما عبر بالابتلاع دون الأكل لأنه عبارة عن إيصال ما يتأتى فيه المضغ وهو لا يتأتى في الحصاة، وكذا كل ما لا يتغذى به ولا يتداوى به كالحجر والتراب والدقيق على الأصح والأرز والعجين والملح إلا إذا اعتاد أكله وحده، ولا في النواة والقطن والكاغد والسفر جل إذا لم يدرك ولا وهو مطبوخ، ولا في ابتلاع الجوزة الرطبة، ويجب لو مضغها أو مضغ اليابسة لا إن ابتلعها وكذا يابس اللوز والبندق والفستق إن ابتلعه لا يجب وإن مضغه وجبت كما يجب في ابتلاع اللوزة الرطبة لأنها تؤكل كما هي الجوزة، وابتلاع التفاحة كاللوزة والرمانة والبيضة كالجوزة وفي ابتلاع البطيخة الصغيرة والخوخة الصغيرة والهليلجة روي عن محمد وجوب الكفارة، وتجب بأكل اللحم النيء وإن بخلاف
And as for when he swallows what is not nourished by nor treated with, like pebbles and iron, the form of breaking the fast exists but no expiation due to the absence of its meaning, which is delivering benefit to the body from the stomach, so the offense is incomplete and it is not obligatory except in its completeness, so it is negated. And in al-Qunyah: He broke the fast in Ramadan repeatedly with dust or clay for the sake of disobedience, then kaffara is upon him as a deterrent. And others wrote, yes the fatwa is according to what the imams of the cities gave fatwa. And he used the term 'swallowing' instead of 'eating' because it is an expression of delivering what in which chewing occurs, and that does not occur in pebbles. Similarly, everything that is not nourished nor treated with, like stone, dust, and flour according to the most sound opinion, and rice, dough, and salt except if he is accustomed to eating it alone, and the pit, cotton, paper, and quince if it is not ripe and not cooked, and swallowing a moist walnut [is not subject to kaffara], but if he chews it or chews the dry one, it is obligatory if he swallows it. Similarly, dry almonds, hazelnuts, pistachios if he swallows them, it is not obligatory; if he chews them, it becomes obligatory. As is the case with swallowing a moist almond because they are eaten as they are like walnuts. Swallowing an apple is like a walnut, a pomegranate and an egg are like a walnut. And regarding swallowing a small watermelon, a small peach, and myrobalan, it is reported from Muhammad that kaffara is obligatory. And it is obligatory by eating raw meat, even if contrary to...
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the ruling on swallowing substances that are not typically eaten or used for medicinal purposes, such as pebbles, iron, clay, etc. The general rule is that swallowing such objects does break the fast (فساد الصوم) because something enters the stomach, but no expiation (كفارة) is required because the essential meaning of breaking the fast—namely, delivering nourishment or beneficial substance to the body—is absent. The author notes an exception from al-Quniyah: if a person deliberately breaks his fast multiple times in Ramadan by swallowing dust or clay as an act of disobedience, then kaffara is required as a deterrent. The passage then provides detailed examples: objects like stones, dust, and flour (on the most sound opinion) break the fast but do not require kaffara. However, if the person is accustomed to eating salt alone, then it is considered like food and requires kaffara. Fruit pits, cotton, paper, and unripe quince that is not cooked are similar. For nuts and fruits, the ruling depends on whether they are chewed or swallowed whole: swallowing a moist walnut or almond whole does not require kaffara, but chewing them does, because chewing makes them like food. Similarly for dry nuts. Swallowing a small watermelon, peach, or myrobalan (هليلجة) is reported from Muhammad to require kaffara. The passage ends with a note on raw meat, where kaffara is obligatory.

Key Terms
الابتلاع (al-ibtila'): Swallowing: causing something to go down the throat into the stomach, distinguished from eating (أكل) which involves chewing.
الكفارة (al-kaffara): Expiation: a specific penalty for breaking the fast intentionally without valid reason, such as fasting two consecutive months or feeding sixty poor people.
الجناية (al-jinaya): The offense: the act of breaking the fast; it is considered a transgression (jinaaya) that requires penalty only when complete.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Kaffara is wajib because the cherry is a fruit.
B) No kaffara because the cherry is not chewed and offers no benefit when swallowed whole.
C) Kaffara is wajib only if the cherry is dried.
D) No kaffara because the cherry is not a food substance.

Key Takeaway
Swallowing non-nutritive objects breaks the fast but does not incur kaffara unless the object is chewed (making it like food) or the act is repeated as disobedience.

Multiple Episodes of Vomiting in Fasting: Abu Yusuf vs Muhammad
Resolving intra-Hanafi dispute on vomiting multiplicity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Abu Yusuf's session-based criterion and Muhammad's cause-based criterion for counting multiple vomiting episodes during fasting, and apply each to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf counts the session; Muhammad counts the cause — the first vomit breaks the fast.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tackles an intra-Hanafi dispute: when a fasting person intentionally vomits multiple times, does the fast break from the first time or only after repeated acts in separate sessions? The resolution hinges on whether we consider the 'session' (majlis) or the 'cause' (sabab). Understanding this distinction sharpens your ability to apply Hanafi usul principles to fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognise that the passage deals with a fasting person who intentionally vomits in multiple sessions or at different times of the day.
The phrase 'كان في مجالس' indicates multiple separate gatherings.
2
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf holds that the session (majlis) unifies multiple acts; multiple vomits in one session count as one cause, so the fast breaks only after the first instance (if it breaks at all).
This is based on the ruling in Khizanat al-Akmal.
3
State Muhammad's position
Muhammad holds that each deliberate act of vomiting is a separate cause (sabab), so the first instance breaks the fast, and subsequent acts occur while not fasting.
He analogises to wudu nullification: each cause breaks wudu regardless of session.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if a person vomits intentionally once in the morning, then again at midday, according to Abu Yusuf the first does not necessarily break the fast (if considered one session?), but according to Muhammad the fast is broken from the morning vomit.
The text says 'يبطل صومه بالمرة الأولى' on Muhammad's view.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The passage notes that Naqd declares Muhammad's view as 'sahih', but Khizanah follows Abu Yusuf. The mufta bihi is not explicit; later Hanafi preference may lean towards Muhammad.
Consider the authority of the sources cited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان في مجالس أو غدوة ثم نصف النهار ثم عشية لا يلزمه كذا في خزانة الأكمل. وتعبيرى بالاستقاء أولى من التعبير بالقيء كما في الشرح . ذلك وبه وينبغي أن يعتبر عند محمد اتحاد السبب لا المجلس كما في نقض الوضوء وأن يكون هو الصحيح كما في النقض، وينبغي أن يكون ما في الخزانة مفرعاً على قول أبي يوسف، أما على قول محمد فإنه يبطل صومه بالمرة الأولى.
If he [vomited] in multiple sessions or once in the morning, then at midday, then in the evening, it does not obligate him? Thus in Khizanat al-Akmal. And my expression 'bi al-istiqa'' is better than the expression 'bi al-qay'' as in the Sharh. That and with it. And it should be considered according to Muhammad that the unity of cause [matters], not the session, as in the nullification of wudu', and that it is the correct view as in the Naqd. And it should be that what is in the Khizanah is branched upon the saying of Abu Yusuf. As for according to Muhammad, he invalidates his fast from the first time.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario where a fasting person vomits intentionally multiple times in different sessions. The author clarifies that the relied-upon expression is 'istiqa'' (seeking to vomit) rather than 'qay'' (vomiting itself). The key legal point is the difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. According to Muhammad, the cause (the deliberate act of vomiting) must be the same; multiple separate causes each break the fast. So the first time he vomits, his fast breaks. According to Abu Yusuf, what matters is the session (majlis); if he vomits multiple times in one session, it is considered one cause. The Khizanat al-Akmal's ruling is based on Abu Yusuf's view. This mirrors the ruling on nullification of wudu by vomiting.

Key Terms
الاستقاء (al-istiqa'): Deliberately inducing vomiting; the act of seeking to vomit.
اتحاد السبب (ittihad al-sabab): The unity of cause: the principle that multiple occurrences of the same cause are treated as one continuous act if they stem from the same cause.
المجلس (al-majlis): The session or gathering; used here to denote a single continuous period of time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Abu Hanifa
B) Abu Yusuf
C) Muhammad al-Shaybani
D) Zufar

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a fasting person intentionally vomits multiple times, Muhammad's view that the first vomit breaks the fast is preferred, while Abu Yusuf's session-based criterion is a valid alternative.

Swallowing Inedible Objects and Detailed Rulings on Various Substances
Explaining the Hanafi ruling on swallowing inedible objects with detailed examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on swallowing inedible objects and apply the distinction between surat al-fitr and ma'nahu to determine whether kaffarah is required for specific substances.

Core Idea
Swallowing inedible objects breaks the fast but requires no kaffarah; kaffarah applies only when the act provides benefit to the body.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq tackles a practical problem: what if a fasting person swallows something that is not food or medicine, like a pebble? The Hanafi school makes a nuanced distinction between the form of breaking fast (merely swallowing) and the intended benefit (nourishment/medicine). This lesson unpacks that legal principle and then applies it to a wide range of specific substances, from nuts and fruits to dirt and paper.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Principle
Locate the opening statement: swallowing inedible objects (e.g., pebble, iron) invalidates the fast (qada) but does not require kaffarah because the act lacks the intended benefit to the body.
The key terms are surat al-fitr (form of breaking fast) and ma'nahu (meaning/benefit).
2
Understand the Proof (Ta'lil)
The text explains that kaffarah requires a complete offense (kamaal al-jinaayah), which requires benefit reaching the body. The absence of benefit makes the offense incomplete.
This is a rational proof (ta'lil) based on the purpose of kaffarah.
3
Recognize the Exception for Repeated Sin
Al-Qunyah reports that if one repeatedly breaks fast by eating dirt out of sin, kaffarah is added as a deterrent. The fatwa of the leading imams supports this.
This exception is based on the deterrent purpose (zajran).
4
Apply the Principle to Specific Items
Work through the examples: stones, dirt, flour, rice, dough, salt (habitual), nuts (wet vs. dry, chewed vs. swallowed), fruits, etc. The ruling changes based on habit and preparation.
Wet walnut swallowed whole: no kaffarah; if chewed: kaffarah. Dry almonds swallowed: no kaffarah; if chewed: kaffarah.
5
Distinguish Based on Custom and Benefit
Items customarily eaten raw (like wet walnut, fresh fruit) or used for medicine (like myrobalan) may require kaffarah even if swallowed whole, because benefit is customarily expected.
The habitual eating of salt or dirt also changes the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا ابتلع ما لا يتغذى به ولا يتداوى به كالحصاة والحديد فلوجود صورة الفطر ولا كفارة لعدم معناه وهو إيصال ما فيه نفع البدن إلى الجوف فقصرت الجناية وهي لا تجب إلا بكمالها فانتفت وفي القنية : أفطر في رمضان مرة بعد أخرى بتراب أو مدر لأجل المعصية فعليه الكفارة زجراً ،له وكتب غيره نعم الفتوى على أفتى أئمة الأمصار وإنما عبر بالابتلاع دون الأكل لأنه عبارة عن إيصال ما يتأتى فيه المضغ وهو لا يتأتى في الحصاة، وكذا كل ما لا يتغذى به ولا يتداوى به كالحجر والتراب والدقيق على الأصح والأرز والعجين والملح إلا إذا اعتاد أكله وحده، ولا في النواة والقطن والكاغد والسفر جل إذا لم يدرك ولا وهو مطبوخ، ولا في ابتلاع الجوزة الرطبة، ويجب لو مضغها أو مضغ اليابسة لا إن ابتلعها وكذا يابس اللوز والبندق والفستق إن ابتلعه لا يجب وإن مضغه وجبت كما يجب في ابتلاع اللوزة الرطبة لأنها تؤكل كما هي الجوزة، وابتلاع التفاحة كاللوزة والرمانة والبيضة كالجوزة وفي ابتلاع البطيخة الصغيرة والخوخة الصغيرة والهليلجة روي عن محمد وجوب الكفارة
As for if he swallows what is not nourished by nor medicine like a pebble and iron, then because the form of breaking fast exists, there is no kaffarah due to the lack of its meaning, which is reaching what has benefit to the body into the stomach, so the offense is limited, and it is not obligated except with its completeness, so it is negated. And in al-Qunyah: He broke fast in Ramadan one after another with dirt or mud due to sin, then upon him is the kaffarah as a deterrent. And others wrote: Yes, the fatwa is upon the fatwa of the leading imams of the cities. And he expressed with 'swallowing' instead of 'eating' because it refers to reaching what can be chewed, and that is not possible in a pebble. And similarly, everything that is not nourished by nor medicine like stone, dirt, and flour on the most correct, and rice, dough, and salt unless he is accustomed to eating it alone, and not in the pit, cotton, paper, and quince if not ripe and not cooked, and not in swallowing a wet walnut; it is obligatory if he chews it or chews the dry one, not if he swallows it. And similarly dry almonds, pistachios, and hazelnuts: if he swallows them it is not obligatory, and if he chews them it is obligatory, as is obligatory in swallowing the wet walnut because it is eaten as it is, like the walnut. And swallowing an apple like the walnut, a pomegranate, and an egg like the walnut. And regarding swallowing a small watermelon, small peach, and myrobalan, it is narrated from Muhammad the obligation of kaffarah.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage begins by distinguishing two categories: objects that have no nutritional or medicinal value (like pebbles, iron) vs. those that do. Swallowing the former breaks the fast (qada), but does not require kaffarah because the act lacks the intended benefit of reaching the body. The author then lists numerous items and their rulings. Key points: 1) If a person habitually eats something inedible (like dirt or salt without food), kaffarah may apply. 2) For nuts and fruits, the ruling depends on whether the item is usually eaten raw or after preparation. Wet walnuts and fresh fruit require kaffarah if swallowed because they are typically eaten as is. Dry items like almonds and pistachios require kaffarah only if chewed, not if swallowed whole. 3) The riwayah of Muhammad on small watermelon and myrobalan indicates kaffarah, likely because these are sometimes used for medicine. The overall principle: kaffarah is due when the act is a complete offense: swallowing something beneficial intentionally.

Key Terms
صورة الفطر (surat al-fitr): The apparent form of breaking fast, i.e., the act of putting something into the stomach.
معناه (ma'nahu): The intended benefit or purpose of nourishment/medicine reaching the body.
أفتى أئمة الأمصار (afta a'immat al-amsar): The fatwa of the leading imams of the cities, referring to the consensus of the major Hanafi scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a fasting person intentionally swallows a small pebble?
A) The fast is valid and no compensation needed.
B) The fast breaks and qada is required, but no kaffarah.
C) Qada and kaffarah are both required.
D) Nothing because pebble is not food.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, kaffarah for breaking fast requires a complete offense: the act must deliver benefit to the body, not merely the form of swallowing.
