Four Kaffarahs Requiring Consecutive Fasts
Listing four kaffarahs requiring consecutive fasts and those that do not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four kaffarahs whose fasts must be consecutive and explain the principle that determines consecutiveness.

Core Idea
A kaffarah fast is consecutive only if freeing a slave is also a valid option.

The Hanafi school distinguishes between kaffarahs that require consecutive fasting and those that do not. This classification hinges on a single principle: whether or not the atonement originally permitted the freeing of a slave. Understanding this principle allows you to correctly categorize any kaffarah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four kaffarahs
From the passage, list the four kaffarahs for which fasting must be consecutive.
They are: breaking Ramadan fast, intentional killing, zihar, and breaking an oath.
2
Note the exceptions
Identify the compensatory fasts that do not require consecutiveness.
These include qada of Ramadan, tamattu', shaving the head, and game penalty.
3
Grasp the principle
Understand the rule: if the kaffarah originally allows freeing a slave, then the fasting option is consecutive; otherwise it is not.
The Arabic criterion is 'shuri'a fiha 'itq' (freeing a slave legislated).
4
Apply the principle
Given a new kaffarah scenario, determine if the fast must be consecutive.
E.g., kaffarah for breaking an oath: freeing a slave is an option, so fast is consecutive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا صوم كفارة اليمين متتابع فهي أربعة بخلاف قضاء رمضان وصوم المتعة وكفارة الحلق وكفارة جزاء الصيد فإنه غير متتابع والأصل أن كل كفارة شرع فيها عتق فإن صومه متتابع، وما لم يشرع فيها عتق فهو مخير كذا في النهاية
And similarly, the fast of kaffarah for an oath is consecutive; so they are four [kaffarahs with consecutive fasts]. Contrary to qada of Ramadan, the fast of tamattu', kaffarah for shaving [the head], and kaffarah for game penalty, for they are not consecutive. The principle is: every kaffarah in which freeing a slave has been legislated, its fast is consecutive; and that in which freeing a slave has not been legislated, it is a choice [i.e., not necessarily consecutive]. This is as stated in al-Nihayah.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage enumerates the kaffarahs whose fasts must be consecutive: (1) Kaffarah for breaking a Ramadan fast (freeing slave/fasting/feeding), (2) Kaffarah for intentional killing (diyat cases), (3) Kaffarah for zihar, (4) Kaffarah for breaking an oath. In all these, if one chooses to fast, it must be consecutive. The other types of kaffarah or compensatory fasts do not require consecutiveness: qada of Ramadan, tamattu' hajj, kaffarah for shaving head during ihram, and kaffarah for game penalty. The underlying principle is that if the kaffarah originally allows freeing a slave (i.e., atonement can be achieved by freeing), then the fasting option must be consecutive. If no freeing option exists (e.g., tamattu' where only fasting is required), then consecutiveness is not required.

Key Terms
مخير (mukhayyar): Free to choose; not bound to consecutiveness
شرع فيها عتق (shuri'a fiha 'itq): Freeing a slave was legislated as a means of atonement in that kaffarah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following requires consecutive fasting according to the passage?
A) Qada of missed Ramadan days
B) Fasting for tamattu' during Hajj
C) Kaffarah for breaking an oath
D) Kaffarah for shaving the head in ihram

Key Takeaway
The consecutiveness of a kaffarah fast depends solely on whether freeing a slave is among its atonement options.

Intention for Qada Fasts: Same or Different Ramadans
Guiding the intention when making up missed fasts from one or two Ramadans

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on intention (niyyah) for qada fasts from the same or different Ramadans and state when specification is required.

Core Idea
Intention for qada fasts is valid even without specifying the exact day or Ramadan when the obligation is similar.

When making up missed fasts, intention (niyyah) is a necessary part of the act. But how specific must that intention be? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that if the missed days are from the same Ramadan, a general intention suffices. For days from different Ramadans, the correct view in the Hanafi school is that even without specification, the fast is accepted. Let's unpack the reasoning and the difference of opinion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes two cases: days owed from the same Ramadan vs. days owed from two different Ramadans.
2
State the ruling for same Ramadan
For two days from the same Ramadan, the person should intend the first obligatory day. If they do not specify, the fast is still valid.
The text says 'جاز' (it is permissible).
3
State the ruling for different Ramadans
For days from different Ramadans, one should intend the first Ramadan. If not, scholars differed, but the correct view (as-sahih) is that it suffices (الإجزاء).
This position is the relied-upon fatwa.
4
Understand the basis of the ruling
The flexibility arises because the missed fasts are of the same type (qada of Ramadan). The legal cause for requiring specificity is weak when the obligations are similar.
This reflects the Hanafi principle that intention can be general when the acts are of the same kind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا وجب عليه قضاء يومين من رمضان واحد ينوي أول يوم وجب عليه وإن لم ينو جاز، وإن كانا من رمضانين ينوي قضاء رمضان الأول، فإن لم ينو ذلك اختلف المشايخ فيه والصحيح الإجزاء
And if making up two days from one Ramadan is obligatory upon him, he intends the first day that is obligatory upon him, and if he does not intend, it is permissible. If they are from two different Ramadans, he intends to make up the first Ramadan. If he does not intend that, the scholars differ on it, and the correct view is that it suffices.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage addresses intention (niyyah) when making up multiple missed fasts. If the days are from the same Ramadan, it is sufficient to intend the first obligatory day; even without specification, the fast is valid because they are of the same type. If the days are from two different Ramadans, one should intend the earlier Ramadan. If the intention is not specific, there is a difference of opinion among scholars, but the correct view (as-sahih) is that it still suffices (i.e., the fast is accepted). This shows flexibility in the Hanafi school regarding intention when the obligation is similar.

Key Terms
ينوي أول يوم وجب عليه (yanwi awwal yawm wajaba 'alayh): He intends the first day that became obligatory upon him (the first missed day)
الإجزاء (al-ijzaa): Sufficiency; that the fast fulfills the obligation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person owes qada for one day from Ramadan 1444 and one day from Ramadan 1445, and he fasts without specifying which Ramadan, what is the ruling according to the correct view?
A) The fast is invalid because he must specify the earlier Ramadan
B) The fast is valid and suffices for one of the missed days
C) He must fast two separate days with specific intentions
D) The fast counts for the later Ramadan if he intends that

Key Takeaway
For qada fasts from the same or even different Ramadans, a general intention to make up missed fasts is sufficient according to the correct Hanafi view.

No Kaffarah for Inzal and Non-Ramadan Fast; Consecutive Fasting Rules
Ruling statement on kaffarah exceptions and consecutive fasting rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two cases where kaffarah is not required according to Hanafi fiqh, and explain the rules of consecutive fasting in kaffarah, including the exception for menstruation and the principle linking emancipation to consecutiveness.

Core Idea
Kaffarah is exclusive to intentionally invalidated Ramadan fasts; other fasts need only qada. Consecutive fasts restart if broken, except for valid excuses like menstruation.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq clarifies two important exceptions to kaffarah obligation and explains the strict requirement of consecutiveness in certain kaffarah fasts. Understanding these rulings prevents misapplication of kaffarah and ensures correct practice when making up missed fasts.

Guided Path
1
Identify cases without kaffarah
From the initial statement, note that kaffarah is not required for inzal from below the private parts and for invalidating a non-Ramadan fast.
Inzal from below faraj invalidates the fast but does not require kaffarah.
2
Understand the effect of breaking a consecutive period
If a fast is broken during a consecutive kaffarah period, all preceding valid fasts are nullified and the entire period must be restarted, regardless of excuse.
The phrase 'سواء أفطر لعذر أو لا' emphasizes that no excuse prevents nullification.
3
Note the exception for menstruation
A woman who menstruates during the consecutive period does not lose her progress; after purification she may continue where she left off. If she does not continue, she restarts.
This is due to the impossibility of fasting during menstruation.
4
List the kaffarah requiring consecutiveness
Consecutive fasting is required for kaffarah of killing, zihar, and oath (three consecutive days).
These are distinguished from other kaffarah like shaving and hunting.
5
State the principle linking emancipation to consecutiveness
Any kaffarah that originally permitted emancipation (e.g., zihar, killing) has a consecutive fast; those that never permitted emancipation do not.
This principle helps remember which fasts require consecutiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم باب ما يفسد الصوم وما لا يفسده ولا كفارة بالإنزال فيما دون الفرج وبإفساد صوم غير رمضان وإن احتقن أو استعط أو أفطر يوماً في خلال المدة بطل ما قبله ولزمه الاستقبال، سواء أفطر لعذر أو لا. وكذا في كفارة القتل والظهار للنص على التتابع إلا لعذر الحيض لأنها لا تجد شهرين عادة لا تحيض فيهما لكنها إذا تطهرت تصل بما مضى فإن لم تصل استقبلت كذا في الولوالجية. وكذا صوم كفارة اليمين متتابع فهي أربعة بخلاف قضاء رمضان وصوم المتعة وكفارة الحلق وكفارة جزاء الصيد فإنه غير متتابع والأصل أن كل كفارة شرع فيها عتق فإن صومه متتابع، وما لم يشرع فيها عتق فهو مخير كذا في النهاية
And no kaffarah for emission from below the private parts, and by invalidating a non-Ramadan fast. And if he inserted a suppository or insufflation or broke a day during the period, what preceded is invalidated and he must restart, whether he broke due to an excuse or not. Likewise in the kaffarah of killing and zihar due to the text on consecutiveness, except for the excuse of menstruation because she cannot find two months during which she is not menstruating normally, but when she becomes pure, she joins what has passed, and if she does not join, she restarts. And likewise the fast of kaffarah of an oath is consecutive, so it is four, unlike the make-up of Ramadan and the fast of tamattu' and the kaffarah of shaving and the kaffarah of hunting, for it is not consecutive. And the principle is that every kaffarah in which emancipation is prescribed has its fast consecutive, and that in which emancipation is not prescribed is optional.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage states several key rulings from the matn. First: No kaffarah is required for inzal (emission) from contact with something other than the private parts (e.g., thigh, armpit), because it is not actual intercourse. However, the fast is still invalidated due to the presence of its meaning (i.e., arousal). Second: Invalidating a non-Ramadan fast (like a voluntary fast or a make-up fast from a different Ramadan) does not require kaffarah, only qada. Third: If one breaks a fast during a consecutive period (like the two months of kaffarah for zihar or killing), the entire preceding valid fasts are lost and he must restart, regardless of excuse. Fourth: Exception for menstruation: a woman who becomes pure continues from where she left off; if she does not continue, she restarts. Fifth: Kaffarah for an oath requires three consecutive days (the text says 'four' but likely a typo for 'three'? Standard Hanafi: oath kaffarah is three consecutive days). In any case, the principle distinguishes between kaffarah that includes emancipation (like zihar and killing) which require consecutive fasting, and those that do not (like shaving, hunting) where fasting is optional.

Key Terms
الإنزال (al-inzal): Emission of seminal fluid, specifically from sexual arousal without full intercourse.
الفرج (al-faraj): The private parts; in this context, the term includes both anterior and posterior passages, though linguistically it refers to the genitals.
التتابع (al-tatābu'): Consecutiveness; the requirement that fasts be performed on consecutive days without interruption.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when is kaffarah NOT required?
A) For inzal from below the private parts
B) For invalidating a non-Ramadan fast
C) Both A and B
D) For breaking a fast during a consecutive period

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah is not required for inzal from below the private parts or for breaking a non-Ramadan fast; consecutive fasts must be restarted if broken, with the exception of menstruation.

Intention When Combining Qada and Voluntary Fasts
Intra-Hanafi dispute on combining qada and voluntary fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the effect of intending both qada and voluntary fast, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Intending two fasts together: Abu Yusuf says make-up by istihsan; Muhammad says voluntary by qiyas.

When a person combines a missed obligatory fast (qada) with a voluntary fast (nafl) in a single intention, which fast takes effect? This question arises in daily practice for those with multiple missed days. The Hanafi masters Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani differ, and their disagreement is rooted in different methods of reasoning: istihsan versus qiyas. This lesson unpacks their proofs and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
The passage covers three situations: two missed days from the same Ramadan, two days from different Ramadans, and combining qada with kaffarah (expiation) fasts. Note the general rulings for each.
2
Understand the default rule for intention
For a valid fast, the intention must specify the type of fast. However, if unspecified, the fast may still be valid under certain conditions – this is the context before the dispute.
3
Locate the dispute
The core disagreement is when one intends both qada and nafl simultaneously. Abu Yusuf holds it counts as qada (istihsan), Muhammad holds it counts as nafl (qiyas). Identify the opposing proofs.
The passage also analogizes to prayer, where intending both results in the obligatory one.
4
Analyze the proofs
Abu Yusuf uses istihsan: the predominant need (ghalaba) for qada makes it the effective intention. Muhammad uses qiyas: since the nafl intention is valid on its own, and both are possible, the fast becomes nafl.
The istihsan is based on the fact that the default obligation is more likely intended.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text states that the relied-upon position is that of Abu Yusuf, as recorded in al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah and considered sound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا وجب عليه قضاء يومين من رمضان واحد ينوي أول يوم وجب عليه وإن لم ينو جاز، وإن كانا من رمضانين ينوي قضاء رمضان الأول، فإن لم ينو ذلك اختلف المشايخ فيه والصحيح الإجزاء ولو صام الفقير إحدى وستين يوماً للكفارة ولم يعين اليوم للقضاء جاز ذلك - كذا ذكره الفقيه أبو الليث - وصار كأنه نوى في اليوم الأول وستين يوماً عن الكفارة كذا في الفتاوى الظهيرية. وعلله في التجنيس بأن الغالب أن الذي يصوم القضاء والكفارة يبدأ بالقضاء وفيه إشكال للمحقق مذكور في فتح القدير. ولو نوى قضاء رمضان والتطوع كان عن القضاء في قول أبي يوسف خلافاً لمحمد فإن عنده يصير شارعاً في التطوع بخلاف الصلاة فإنه إذا نوى التطوع والفرض القضاء لا الصلاة أصلاً شارعاً في يصير القضاء استحساناً، وفي القياس يكون تطوعاً وهو قول محمد. كذا في الفتاوى الظهيرية.
And if he owes the make-up of two days from one Ramadan, he intends the first day and it is obligatory upon him, and if he does not intend, it is valid. And if they are from two Ramadans, he intends the make-up of the first Ramadan; if he does not intend that, the scholars differ, and the sound view is that it is valid. And if a poor person fasts sixty-one days for kaffarah and does not specify the day for make-up, it is valid. And it becomes as if he intended on the first day sixty days for kaffarah. And it is reasoned in al-Tajnis that the one who fasts make-up and kaffarah usually starts with make-up, and there is a difficulty mentioned in Fath al-Qadir. And if he intends make-up of Ramadan and supererogatory, it counts as make-up according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad, because according to him he becomes a starter of supererogatory, unlike prayer, because if he intends supererogatory and obligatory in prayer, he does not become a starter of prayer at all; by istihsan he becomes a starter of make-up, and by qiyas it would be supererogatory, which is Muhammad's view.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage addresses the complex issue of niyyah when multiple obligations coincide. For two missed days from the same Ramadan, one must intend the first day; if unspecified, the fast is valid anyway. For two different Ramadans, intention of the older Ramadan is needed; if unspecified, the correct position is validity. The principle of ghara (predominant type) applies: one who fasts many days usually begins with qada. The main dispute is between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on whether intending both qada and nafl counts as qada or nafl. Abu Yusuf says qada by istihsan; Muhammad says nafl by qiyas. The mufta bihi is Abu Yusuf's view according to the text.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intention in the heart to perform a specific act of worship; in fasting, it determines which fast is being observed.
قضاء (qada'): The make-up of a missed obligatory act, here specifically the make-up of missed days of Ramadan.
تطوع (tatawwu'): Voluntary or supererogatory act of worship.
غالب (ghalaba): Predominant practice or need; used in istihsan to determine which intention prevails.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that deviates from strict analogy for a more equitable result.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning; extending a ruling from an original case to a new case due to shared effective cause (illa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The fast is invalid
B) It is valid if he intended the first day
C) It is valid even without specifying
D) It counts as make-up for the earlier day

Key Takeaway
When intending both qada and nafl, the Hanafi mufta bihi (Abu Yusuf) holds that the fast counts as qada by istihsan, not as nafl by qiyas.

The Effect of Tawbah on Kaffarah for Deliberately Breaking Ramadan
Clarifies that tawbah does not remove the obligation of kaffarah for deliberate Ramadan breaking, with a refined distinction between inward forgiveness and outward legal duty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that tawbah does not remove the obligation of kaffarah for deliberately breaking a Ramadan fast and explain the distinction between hadd and kaffarah regarding the effect of repentance.

Core Idea
Tawbah removes the sin between you and Allah, but it does not erase the obligation of kaffarah — the outward legal duty remains.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq refines an earlier ruling on deliberate eating in Ramadan. It clarifies that while sincere repentance purifies the soul and restores the relationship with Allah, the prescribed expiation (kaffarah) remains an obligatory outward duty. The author also compares this to hadd punishments and addresses a potential objection, distinguishing between financial obligations and penal penalties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Identify what the passage is discussing: deliberate eating during the day in Ramadan. The passage first notes that if done openly with desire, it indicates istihlal (deeming lawful) and warrants execution. Then it focuses on the sin itself: deliberate breaking of the fast.
The execution is for those who openly consider it lawful, which is an act of apostasy.
2
Locate the dalil for the effect of tawbah
The passage cites al-Hidayah: 'By imposing emancipation, it is known that repentance does not expiate this crime.' This is the primary evidence that kaffarah remains obligatory even after repentance.
Al-Hidayah is a core Hanafi text; its ruling is relied upon.
3
Understand the two dimensions of removal
The passage distinguishes between outward (zahir) and inward (baynahu wa bayna Rabbihi). Inwardly, tawbah removes the sin, but outwardly (i.e., the legal obligation of kaffarah) it does not.
This parallels the hadd of zina: tawbah removes the sin before Allah but does not prevent the judge from executing the hadd.
4
Examine the analogy to theft and zina
The passage compares deliberate fasting break to theft and zina: mere repentance does not remove the hadd penalty. Similarly, repentance does not remove the kaffarah obligation. However, unlike hadd, kaffarah is a financial obligation, not a corporal punishment.
The analogy helps understand why tawbah does not cancel outward legal consequences.
5
Address the critical refinement by al-Ramli
Al-Ramli raises a potential inconsistency: if tawbah removes the sin, why doesn't it also remove the kaffarah? The passage resolves this by noting that kaffarah is a property right (right of Allah in the form of financial expiation) that is not waived by tawbah, unlike the personal sin.
This refinement deepens the understanding of the distinction between spiritual forgiveness and legal obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الفتاوى البزازية : من أكل نهاراً في رمضان عياناً عمداً شهوة يقتل لأنه دليل الاستحلال اهـ. واعلم أن هذا الذنب - أعني ذنب الإفطار عمداً - لا يرتفع بالتوبة بل لا بد من التكفير ولهذا قال في الهداية وبإيجاب الإعتاق عرف أن التوبة غير مكفرة لهذه الجناية. وتبعه الشارحون، وشبهه في غاية البيان بجناية السرقة والزنا حيث لا يرتفعان بمجرد التوبة بل يرتفعان بالحد، وهذا يقتضي أن المراد بعدم الارتفاع عدمه ظاهراً، أما فيما بينه وبين ربه فيرتفع بالتوبة بدون تكفير لأن حد الزنا يرتفع فيما بينه وبين الله بالتوبة كما صرحوا به. وأما القاضي بعد ما رفع الزاني إليه لا يقبل منه التوبة بل يقيم الحد عليه وقد صرح الشيخ زكريا عنده ولو نوي قضاء رمضان وكفارة الظهار كان عن يلزم أن تكون مثلها من كل وجه فإن المسيس في أثنائها يقطع التتابع في كفارة الظهار مطلقاً عمداً أو نسياناً، ليلاً أو نهاراً للآية، بخلاف كفارة الصوم والقتل فإنه لا يقطعه فيهما إلا الفطر بعذر أو بغير عذر فتأمل. فقد زلت بعض الأقدام في هذا المقام (رملي). قوله : (إما فيما بينه وبين ربه فيرتفع بالتوبة بدون تكفير فيه أنه يلزمه أن تسقط الكفارة بالتوبة أيضاً، ويدل على هذا اللزوم كلام الهداية فإنه جعل إيجاب الإعتاق معرف رفاً لعدم تكفير التوبة للذنب فإن مفاده أنه لو كفرته لم يجب مال فالظاهر الفرق بين الحدود والكفارات فليتأمل قوله : (لأن حد الزنا يرتفع) قال أبو السعود محشي مسكين قيده في بحر الكلام بما إذا لم يكن للمزني بها زوج فإن
And in al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyya: Whoever eats during the day in Ramadan openly, intentionally, out of desire, is killed because it is evidence of considering it lawful. End. And know that this sin - meaning the sin of deliberate breaking - is not removed by repentance, but rather kaffarah is necessary. For this reason it is said in al-Hidayah: 'By imposing emancipation, it is known that repentance does not expiate this crime.' And the commentators followed him, and it is compared in Ghayat al-Bayan to the crime of theft and adultery, which are not removed by mere repentance but by hadd. And this implies that the meaning of non-removal is non-removal outwardly. As for what is between him and his Lord, it is removed by repentance without kaffarah, because the hadd of adultery is removed between him and Allah by repentance as they stated. And as for the judge, after the adulterer is brought to him, he does not accept his repentance but establishes the hadd upon him. And Shaykh Zakariyya stated with him, and if he intended make-up of Ramadan and kaffarah of zihar, it would be necessary that it be like it in every respect, because intercourse during them cuts the consecutiveness in kaffarah of zihar absolutely, intentionally or unintentionally, night or day due to the verse, unlike the kaffarah of fasting and killing, for it does not cut it except by breaking with or without excuse. So ponder. Some feet have slipped in this matter (al-Ramli). (He said:) 'As for between him and his Lord, it is removed by repentance without kaffarah, it implies that kaffarah should also be dropped by repentance, and this necessity is indicated by the wording of al-Hidayah, for it made the imposition of emancipation a recognition that repentance does not expiate the sin, implying that if it did expiate, then property would not be obligatory. So apparently there is a difference between hadd and kaffarah. So ponder. (He said:) 'Because the hadd of adultery is removed' - Abu al-Sa'ud, the marginal annotator of Miskin, said in the Sea of Speech that it is restricted to when the woman has no husband, for
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the earlier ruling. It begins with a quote from al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyya that one who eats openly in Ramadan with desire is killed, as it indicates istihlal (deeming it lawful). Then it discusses the nature of the sin: deliberate breaking of fast is a major sin that requires kaffarah (expiation) and is not removed by mere repentance. However, there is a subtlety: the non-removal is outward (zahiran) regarding the legal consequences; inwardly, between the servant and Allah, repentance does remove the sin. The author then compares to hadd punishments like zina, where tawbah does not prevent execution of hadd by the judge, but between Allah and the servant it removes the sin. The passage includes a critique by al-Ramli, pointing out that if tawbah removes the sin, then by analogy it should also remove the obligation of kaffarah, but the texts indicate otherwise. The key distinction is between hadd (which is a right of Allah in the sense of punishment) and kaffarah (which is a financial obligation). The passage ends with a note on a different issue about kaffarah of zihar and consecutiveness.

Key Terms
التوبة (al-tawbah): Repentance: turning back from sin with regret, cessation, and resolve not to return.
التكفير (al-takfīr): Expiation: the prescribed action (e.g., freeing a slave, fasting, feeding) that must be performed to atone for a specific sin.
الاستحلال (al-istiḥlāl): Deeming something lawful that is known by necessity to be unlawful; leading to apostasy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Hadd is removed by tawbah outwardly but kaffarah is not
C) Hadd is removed between Allah and the servant by tawbah, but kaffarah is not removed by tawbah
D) Both are removed by tawbah between Allah and the servant

Key Takeaway
Deliberately breaking a Ramadan fast requires kaffarah regardless of repentance; tawbah removes the sin between the servant and Allah but not the legal obligation.

Inserting Medicine Through Orifices or Wounds Invalidates the Fast
Explains the ruling that inserting medicine into the ear or a wound breaks the fast, supported by the hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that inserting medicine into the ear, nose, anus, or a penetrating wound breaks the fast, and cite the hadith proof that fasting is broken by what enters, not what exits.

Core Idea
Fasting is broken by anything that enters the body; the hadith 'al-fitr mimma dakhala' is the proof.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rai'iq explains a key principle in Hanafi fiqh of sawm: that inserting medicine or other substances through any orifice or wound invalidates the fast. The author cites the famous hadith 'al-fitr mimma dakhala' (breaking the fast is from what enters) as evidence. We will unpack the ruling, its proof, and the exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts that break the fast
List the specific acts mentioned: inserting drops in the ear, using a suppository, insufflating medicine (snuff), and treating a penetrating wound (ja'ifah) or head wound (ammah) with medicine that reaches the interior or brain.
These are all cases where something enters the body through orifices or wounds.
2
Locate the hadith proof
Find the hadith cited: 'al-fitr mimma dakhala wa laysa mimma kharaja' (breaking the fast is from what enters, not from what exits). Note its source: Abu Ya'la al-Mawsili in his Musnad.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but used as evidence by the Hanafi school.
3
Apply the hadith to the cases
Understand that the phrase 'mimma dakhala' (from what enters) is general and includes any substance entering the body through any route, whether oral, nasal, aural, rectal, or through wounds.
The Hanafis do not restrict entry to the digestive tract; any entry into the body cavity or brain suffices.
4
Examine the exceptions
The passage reconciles the hadith with the hadith on cupping (istiga'), where blood exits. It explains that cupping is excluded because it is rare, or that the ruling is based on something returning inside. Also note that menstruation and postnatal bleeding invalidate the fast although they are outflows, but these are exceptional due to legal capacity.
Exceptions are based on other evidence, not on the general principle.
5
Recognize the implication
The general rule is that any intentional entry of a substance into the body invalidates the fast. This includes modern scenarios like inhalers, eye drops, nasal sprays, etc., if they reach the interior.
This is a broader principle than mere eating/drinking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقطر في أذنه أو داوى جائفة أو آمة بدواء ووصل الدواء إلى جوفه أو دماغه أفطر وإن من الشافعية في شرح المنهج بارتفاعه بدون تكفير فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى. وعبر بـ«من» المفيدة للعموم في قوله «من جامع أو جومع» ليفيد أنه لا فرق في الحكم وهو وجوب الكفارة بين الذكر والأنثى والحر والعبد، ولهذا صرح في البزازية بالوجوب على الجارية فيما لو أخبرات سيدها بعد طلوع الفجر عالمة بطلوعه فجامعها مع عدم الوجوب عليه، وكذا لا السلطان بين وغيره ولهذا قال في البزازية : إذا لزم الكفارة على السلطان وهو موسر بما له الحلال وليس عليه تبعة لأحد يفتى بإعتاق الرقبة. وقال أبو نصر محمد بن سلام: يفتي بصيام شهرين لأن المقصود من الكفارة الإنزجار ويسهل عليه إفطار شهر وإعتاق رقبة فلا فرق يحصل الزجر . قوله (ولا كفارة بالإنزال فيما دون الفرج أي في غير القبل والدبر كالفخذ والإبط والبطن لانعدام الجماع صورة وفسد صومه لوجوده معنى كما قدمناه في المباشره والتة .. وعمل المرأتين كذلك كما قدمناه. وفي المغرب : الفرج قبل الرجل والمرأة باتفاق أهل اللغة، وقوله القبل والدبر كلاهما فرج يعني في الحكم اهـ بلفظه يعني لا في اللغة قوله (وبإفساد صوم غير رمضان أي لا كفارة في إفساد صوم غير أداء رمضان لأن الإفطار في رمضان أبلغ في الجناية لهتك حرمة الشهر فلا يلحق به غيره لا قياساً إذا هو ممتنع لكونه على خلاف القياس، ولا دلالة لأن إفساد غيره ليس في معناه ولزوم إفساد الحج النفل والقضاء بالجماع ليس إلحاقاً بإفساد الحج الفرض بل هو ثابت ابتداء لعموم نص القضاء والإجماع. قوله: (وإذا احتقن أو استعط أو أقطر في أذنه أو داوى جائفة أو آمة بدواء ووصل إلى جوفة أو دماغه أفطر) لقوله عليه السلام الفطر مما دخل وليس مما خرج رواه أبو يعلى الموصلي في مسنده وهو مخصوص بحديث الاستقاء، أو الفطر فيه باعتبار أنه يعود شيء وإن قل حتى لا يحس كذا في فتح القدير. فإن قلت ظاهره أن الخارج لا يبطل الصوم أصلاً إلا في الاستقاء، والحصر ممنوع لأن الحيض والنفاس كل منهما يفسد الصوم كما صرح به في البدائع . . قلت : لا يرد لأن إفسادهما الصوم باعتبار منافاتهما الأهلية له شرعاً على خلاف القياس يإجماع الصحابة بخلاف الجنون والإغماء بعد النية لا يفسدان الصوم لأنهما لا ينافيان أهلية الأداء وإنما ينافيان النية. كذا في البدائع والرواية بالفتح في احتقن واستعط أي وضع الحقنة في الدبر وصب السعوط وهو الدواء في الأنف، وبالضم في أقطر. والجائفة اسم الجراحة وصلت إلى الجوف والآمة الجراحة وصلت إلى أم الدماغ. وأطلق في الإفطار في به. كان فلا بد من إعلامه لكونه حق عبد فلا بد من إبرائه عنه قوله : (بالوجوب على الجارية) أي وجوب كفارة الصوم قوله : (أو الفطر فيه أي في الاستقاء قوله : حتى لا يحس به) أي فلا يكون الحديث الأول مخصوصاً بحديث الاستقاء قوله : (وبالضم في أقطر) قال في النهر: قيل الصواب
If he puts drops in his ear, or treats a penetrating wound or a head wound with medicine, and the medicine reaches his interior or his brain, he breaks his fast, even if... from the Shafi'is in Sharh al-Minhaj that it is removed without expiation between him and Allah Ta'ala. And he expressed with 'man' which indicates generality in his statement 'whoever has intercourse or is had intercourse with' to indicate that there is no difference in the ruling, which is the obligation of kaffarah, between male and female, free and slave. Therefore, it is stated in al-Bazzaziyya that it is obligatory on the slave girl if she informed her master after dawn knowing that it had dawned, and he had intercourse with her, despite there being no obligation on him. And likewise, the sultan and others. For this reason it is said in al-Bazzaziyya: If kaffarah is binding on the sultan and he is wealthy from lawful property and has no debt to anyone, fatwa is given to free a slave. And Abu Nasr Muhammad ibn Salam said: He gives fatwa to fast two months, because the purpose of kaffarah is deterrence, and it is easier for him to fast a month and free a slave, so there is no difference in achieving deterrence. (He said:) 'And no kaffarah by emission from below the private parts' meaning other than the anterior and posterior passages, like the thigh, armpit, and belly, due to the absence of actual intercourse in form, and his fast is nullified due to its presence in meaning as we presented in the section on direct contact... And the act of two women is likewise as we presented. And in al-Mughrib: The private parts of a man and a woman are agreed upon by language scholars, and his statement 'the anterior and posterior passages are both private parts' means in legal ruling. (He said:) 'And by invalidating a non-Ramadan fast' meaning no kaffarah in invalidating a non-obligatory Ramadan fast, because breaking fast in Ramadan is more severe in transgression due to the sanctity of the month, so nothing else is joined to it, neither by qiyas because it is impossible as it is contrary to qiyas, nor by indication, because invalidating other fasts is not in its meaning. And the obligation to invalidate the optional hajj and make-up by intercourse is not a joining to invalidating obligatory hajj, but is established from the beginning due to the general text on make-up and consensus. (He said:) 'And if he inserted a suppository, or insufflated, or put drops in his ear, or treated a penetrating wound or a head wound with medicine, and it reached his interior or his brain, he breaks his fast' due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Breaking the fast is from what enters, not from what exits' reported by Abu Ya'la al-Mawsili in his Musnad. And it is specific to the hadith of cupping, or breaking the fast in it is on the basis that it returns something, even if little, so that he does not notice. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. If you say: Its apparent meaning is that that which exits does not invalidate the fast at all except in cupping, and the exclusivity is denied because menstruation and post-childbirth bleeding both invalidate the fast, as stated in al-Bada'i'. I say: It is not valid because these two invalidate the fast due to their conflicting with the capacity for it legally, contrary to qiyas, by consensus of the Companions, unlike insanity and unconsciousness after intention, which do not invalidate the fast because they do not conflict with the capacity to perform, only with the intention. Thus in al-Bada'i'. And the vocalization is with fatha in 'ihtaqana' and 'ista'ata' i.e., placing the enema in the anus and pouring the snuff which is medicine into the nose, and with damma in 'aqtara'. And the penetrating wound is the name for the injury that reaches the interior, and the head wound is the injury that reaches the membrane of the brain. And he spoke broadly about breaking the fast in it. It was necessary to inform him because it is a right of a servant, so it is necessary to clear him from it. (He said:) 'Regarding the obligation on the slave girl' i.e., the obligation of kaffarah of fasting. (He said:) 'Or breaking the fast in it' i.e., in cupping. (He said:) 'So that he does not notice it' i.e., so the first hadith is not specific to the hadith of cupping. (He said:) 'And with damma in aqtar' said in al-Nahr: It is said that the correct...
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on the matn. It begins with a continuation of an earlier discussion about how the Shafi'is view the effect of tawbah on kaffarah, then moves to commentary on the matn's phrases. It clarifies that the ruling on kaffarah applies to both genders and slaves. Then it explains each part of the matn: (1) 'No kaffarah for inzal below the private parts' means that since there is no actual intercourse, but the fast is still invalidated due to arousal. (2) 'By invalidating a non-Ramadan fast' means no kaffarah is required because the sanctity of Ramadan is greater. (3) The main point: inserting anything into the body through the nose, ear, anus, or a wound breaks the fast, based on the hadith 'al-fitr mimma dakhala' (Breaking the fast is from what enters). This hadith is then reconciled with the hadith on cupping (istiga') which allows cupping despite it exiting blood. The author explains that cupping is excepted because it is rare, or that the ruling is based on something returning inside. The passage also addresses a potential objection about menstruation and postnatal bleeding, which break the fast despite being outflow, but these are exceptional due to legal capacity. Finally, it gives linguistic explanations of the terms 'ihtaqana', 'ista'ata', and 'aqtara'.

Key Terms
الجائفة (al-jā'ifah): A wound that penetrates into the body cavity (jauf), such as a wound to the abdomen that reaches the interior.
الآمة (al-āmmah): A wound that reaches the membrane covering the brain (ummu al-dimagh).
السعوط (al-sa'ūṭ): Medicine that is insufflated (snuffed) into the nose.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary evidence that inserting medicine into the ear or a wound breaks the fast?
A) The Quranic verse on fasting
B) Consensus of the Companions
C) The hadith 'al-fitr mimma dakhala'
D) Analogy to eating and drinking

Key Takeaway
Any intentional entry of a substance into the body through an orifice or wound that reaches the interior or brain invalidates the fast, based on the hadith 'al-fitr mimma dakhala'.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
8 min
Invalidation by Entry of Substances and the Principle of al-Fitr mimma dakhala
Commentary linking hadith proof to specific invalidation cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that introducing a substance into the interior through any opening invalidates the fast and apply the hadith 'al-fitr mimma dakhala' as the proof, while distinguishing exceptions like menstruation.

Core Idea
The fast is broken by anything beneficial entering the interior; expiation is only for intercourse in Ramadan.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the core Hanafi principle that the fast is invalidated when a substance enters the interior of the body through any natural opening, supported by the prophetic hadith 'al-fitr mimma dakhala'. The commentator explains the rule's application to enema, snuff, ear drops, and wound treatments, and addresses an apparent contradiction regarding menstruation and postpartum bleeding, which invalidate the fast despite being exits, by distinguishing legal capacity from the general rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The matn states that if a fasting person administers an enema, snuff, ear drops, or treats a deep wound (ja'ifah) or skull wound (ammah) with medicine that reaches the interior or brain, the fast is broken. Recognize that these are all cases of deliberate introduction of a substance.
2
Locate the dalil
The commentator cites the hadith: 'al-fitr mimma dakhala wa laysa mimma kharaja' (breaking the fast is from what enters, not from what exits), narrated by Abu Ya'la. Understand that this is the primary proof for the rule.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) and its meaning is zanni, but it is relied upon in the school.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'mimma dakhala' indicates that anything that enters the body through the natural openings causes iftar if it reaches the interior. The exception for vomiting (exit) is established by another hadith. The negative 'laysa mimma kharaja' means exit alone does not break the fast, but exceptions exist (e.g., menstruation) due to legal incapacity, not the general rule.
4
Note the exception: menstruation and postpartum bleeding
An objection arises: if exit does not invalidate, why does menstruation break the fast? The commentator replies that these invalidate because they conflict with the legal capacity (ahliyyah) for fasting, not because of the rule of entry/exit. This is established by ijma' of the Companions and is contrary to analogy.
This highlights the principle that rulings can have multiple bases.
5
Apply the rule to the listed cases
Enema (ihtiqan), snuff (sa'ut), ear drops (aqtar), and wound treatments all involve introducing medicine (a beneficial substance) into the interior. Since the substance reaches the jowf or brain, the fast is invalidated. No expiation is required unless it is intercourse during Ramadan.
The rule applies specifically to substances that are beneficial ('islah al-badan').

أقطر في أذنه أو داوى جائفة أو آمة بدواء ووصل الدواء إلى جوفه أو دماغه أفطر وإن من الشافعية في شرح المنهج بارتفاعه بدون تكفير فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى. وعبر بـ«من» المفيدة للعموم في قوله «من جامع أو جومع» ليفيد أنه لا فرق في الحكم وهو وجوب الكفارة بين الذكر والأنثى والحر والعبد، ولهذا صرح في البزازية بالوجوب على الجارية فيما لو أخبرات سيدها بعد طلوع الفجر عالمة بطلوعه فجامعها مع عدم الوجوب عليه، وكذا لا السلطان بين وغيره ولهذا قال في البزازية : إذا لزم الكفارة على السلطان وهو موسر بما له الحلال وليس عليه تبعة لأحد يفتى بإعتاق الرقبة. وقال أبو نصر محمد بن سلام: يفتي بصيام شهرين لأن المقصود من الكفارة الإنزجار ويسهل عليه إفطار شهر وإعتاق رقبة فلا فرق يحصل الزجر . قوله (ولا كفارة بالإنزال فيما دون الفرج أي في غير القبل والدبر كالفخذ والإبط والبطن لانعدام الجماع صورة وفسد صومه لوجوده معنى كما قدمناه في المباشره والتة .. وعمل المرأتين كذلك كما قدمناه. وفي المغرب : الفرج قبل الرجل والمرأة باتفاق أهل اللغة، وقوله القبل والدبر كلاهما فرج يعني في الحكم اهـ بلفظه يعني لا في اللغة قوله (وبإفساد صوم غير رمضان أي لا كفارة في إفساد صوم غير أداء رمضان لأن الإفطار في رمضان أبلغ في الجناية لهتك حرمة الشهر فلا يلحق به غيره لا قياساً إذا هو ممتنع لكونه على خلاف القياس، ولا دلالة لأن إفساد غيره ليس في معناه ولزوم إفساد الحج النفل والقضاء بالجماع ليس إلحاقاً بإفساد الحج الفرض بل هو ثابت ابتداء لعموم نص القضاء والإجماع. قوله: (وإذا احتقن أو استعط أو أقطر في أذنه أو داوى جائفة أو آمة بدواء ووصل إلى جوفة أو دماغه أفطر) لقوله عليه السلام الفطر مما دخل وليس مما خرج رواه أبو يعلى الموصلي في مسنده وهو مخصوص بحديث الاستقاء، أو الفطر فيه باعتبار أنه يعود شيء وإن قل حتى لا يحس كذا في فتح القدير. فإن قلت ظاهره أن الخارج لا يبطل الصوم أصلاً إلا في الاستقاء، والحصر ممنوع لأن الحيض والنفاس كل منهما يفسد الصوم كما صرح به في البدائع . . قلت : لا يرد لأن إفسادهما الصوم باعتبار منافاتهما الأهلية له شرعاً على خلاف القياس يإجماع الصحابة بخلاف الجنون والإغماء بعد النية لا يفسدان الصوم لأنهما لا ينافيان أهلية الأداء وإنما ينافيان النية. كذا في البدائع والرواية بالفتح في احتقن واستعط أي وضع الحقنة في الدبر وصب السعوط وهو الدواء في الأنف، وبالضم في أقطر. والجائفة اسم الجراحة وصلت إلى الجوف والآمة الجراحة وصلت إلى أم الدماغ. وأطلق في الإفطار في الأذن فشمل الماء والدهن وهو في الدهن بلا خلاف أما الماء فاختار في الهداية عدم الإفطار به، سواء دخل بنفسه أو أدخله. وصرح الولوالجي بأنه لا يفسد صومه مطلقاً بأنه لم يوجد الفطر صورة ولا معنى لأنه مما لا يتعلق به صلاح البدن بوصوله إلى الدماغ. وجعل السعوط كالإقطار في الأذن وصححه في المحيط . وفي فتاوى قاضيخان أنه إن خاض الماء فدخل أذنه لا يفسد، وإن صب الماء في أذنه فالصحيح أنه يفسد لأنه وصل إلى الجوف بفعله، ورجحه المحقق في فتح القدير. وبهذا يعلم حكم الغسل وهو صائم إذا دخل الماء في أذنه. وفي عمدة الفتاوى للصدر الشهيد : فلو دخل الماء في أنفه أو أذنه ووصل إلى الدماغ لا شيء عليه اهـ. ولو شد الطعام بخيط وأرسله في حلقه وطرف الخيط في يده لا يفسد الصوم إلا إذا انفصل . وذكر الولوالجي أن الصائم إذا استقصى في الاستنجاء حتى بلغ مبلغ المحقنة فهذا أقل ما يكون ولو كان يفسد صومه والاستقصاء لا يفعل لأنه يورث داء عظيماً. وفي الظهيرية : ولو أدخل خشبة أو نحوها وطرفاً منها بيده لم يفسد صومه . قال في البدائع : وهذ يدل على أن استقرار الداخل في الجوف شرط لفساد الصوم ، وكذا لو أدخل أصبعه في أسته أو أدخلت المرأة في فرجها هو المختار إلا إذا كانت الأصبع مبتلة بالماء أو الدهن فحينئذ يفسد لوصول الماء أو الدهن. وقيل: إن المرأة إذا حشت الفرج الداخل فسد صومها، والصائم إذ أصابه سهم وخرج من الجانب الآخر لم يفسد صومه ولو بقي النصل في جوفه يفسد صومه اهـ . وفي شرح الجامع الصغير لقاضيخان : وإن بقي الرمح في جوفه اختلفوا فيه والصحيح أنه لا يفسد لأنه لم يوجد منه الفعل ولم يصل إليه ما فيه صلاحه. وذكر الولوالجي: وأما الوجور في الفم فإنه يفسد صومه لأنه وصل إلى جوف البدن ما هو مصلح للبدن فكان أكلاً معنى لكن لا تلزمه الكفارة لانعدام الأكل صورة. وعن أبي يوسف في السعوط والوجور الكفارة، ولو استعط ليلاً فخرج نهاراً لا يفطر. وأطلق الدواء فشمل الرطب واليابس لأن العبرة للوصول لا لكونه رطباً أو يابساً، وإنما شرطه القدوري لأن الرطب هو الذي يصل إلى الجوف عادة حتى لو علم أن الرطب لم يصل لم يفسد ولو علم أن اليابس وصل فسد صومه . كذا في العناية لكن بقي ما إذا لم يعلم يقيناً أحدهما وكان رطباً فعند أبي حنيفة يفطر للوصول عادة. وقالا : لا
If he puts drops in his ear or treats a deep wound or a skull wound with medicine and the medicine reaches his interior or brain, he breaks his fast. Even from the Shafi'is in Sharh al-Minhaj, that it is lifted without expiation between him and Allah. And he expressed with 'min' indicating generality in his saying 'whoever has intercourse or is had intercourse with' to indicate that there is no difference in the ruling, which is the obligation of expiation, between male and female, free and slave. Thus it is stated in al-Bazzaziyyah that it is obligatory on the slave girl if she informs her master after dawn, knowing it is dawn, and he has intercourse with her despite no obligation on him. Likewise, it does not matter whether he is a sultan or other. Thus he said in al-Bazzaziyyah: If expiation becomes obligatory on the sultan and he is wealthy from lawful means and there is no liability on him towards anyone, he is given the fatwa to free a slave. Abu Nasr Muhammad ibn Salam said: He is given the fatwa to fast two months because the purpose of expiation is deterrence, and it is easy for him to fast a month and free a slave, so there is no difference. He said: (And no expiation for emission below the private part) i.e., other than the front and back, like the thigh, armpit, and belly, because intercourse is absent in form and his fast is invalidated due to its presence in meaning, as we mentioned in direct contact... And the action of two women is likewise as we mentioned. In al-Mughrib: 'Farj' refers to the male and female private parts by agreement of the philologists, and his saying 'front and back' both are 'farj' in legal sense, not linguistically. He said: (And by spoiling a fast other than Ramadan) i.e., no expiation for spoiling a fast other than performing Ramadan, because breaking the fast in Ramadan is more severe in offense due to violating the sanctity of the month, so nothing else is attached to it, neither by analogy—since it is contrary to analogy—nor by indication, because spoiling other fasts is not in its meaning. And the obligation of spoiling supererogatory pilgrimage and make-up by intercourse is not by analogy to spoiling obligatory pilgrimage, but is established initially due to the generality of the text on make-up and consensus. He said: (And if he gives an enema, or takes snuff, or puts drops in his ear, or treats a deep wound or skull wound with medicine and it reaches his interior or brain, he breaks his fast) due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Breaking the fast is from what enters, not from what exits'—narrated by Abu Ya'la al-Mawsili in his Musnad. And it is restricted by the hadith on vomiting, or breaking the fast in it because it returns something even if little, so that he does not perceive it, as in Fath al-Qadir. If you say: Its apparent meaning is that what exits does not invalidate the fast at all except for vomiting, but the restriction is denied because menstruation and postpartum bleeding each invalidate the fast, as stated in al-Bada'i'... I say: That is not rebutted because they invalidate the fast due to their conflicting with legal capacity by consensus of the Companions, contrary to analogy. Unlike insanity and fainting after the intention, they do not invalidate the fast because they do not conflict with capacity for performance but rather conflict with the intention. Thus in al-Bada'i'. The pronunciation is with fatha (open vowel) in 'ihtaqana' and 'ista'ata' i.e., placing the enema in the anus and pouring snuff, which is medicine into the nose, and with dammah (u vowel) in 'aqtara'. 'Ja'ifah' is the name for a wound reaching the interior, and 'ammah' is a wound reaching the brain membrane. He generalized about breaking the fast with the ear, so it includes water and oil—regarding oil there is no disagreement. As for water, al-Hidayah chose that it does not break the fast, whether it enters by itself or he inserts it. And al-Walwaliji stated that it does not break his fast absolutely, because breaking the fast is not found in form nor meaning, since it does not involve benefit to the body by reaching the brain. And he made snuff like drops in the ear, and it was corrected in al-Muhit. In the Fatawa of Qadikhan, if he wades in water and water enters his ear, it does not break; but if he pours water into his ear, the correct view is that it breaks because it reaches the interior by his action, and the scholar in Fath al-Qadir gave preponderance to this. From this, the ruling is known for bathing while fasting if water enters his ear. In 'Umdat al-Fatawa of al-Sadr al-Shahid: 'If water enters his nose or ear and reaches the brain, there is nothing upon him.' And if he ties food with a string and sends it down his throat while holding the string, his fast is not broken unless it detaches. And al-Walwaliji mentioned that if the fasting person is thorough in istinja' until it reaches the level of an enema, then it is the least that it would break his fast, but thoroughness is not done because it causes great disease. In al-Zahiriyyah: If he inserts a stick or similar with one end in his hand, his fast is not broken. Al-Bada'i' said: This indicates that settling of what enters in the interior is a condition for breaking the fast. Likewise, if he inserts his finger into his anus or a woman inserts into her vagina, the chosen view is it does not break unless the finger is wet with water or oil, then it breaks due to water or oil reaching. It is said: If a woman stuffs her vagina with dry matter, her fast is broken. If the fasting person is struck by an arrow and it exits from the other side, his fast is not broken; if the arrowhead remains in his interior, his fast is broken. In Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir of Qadikhan: If the spear remains in his interior, they differed, and the correct view is that it does not break because no action came from him and nothing beneficial reached him. Al-Walwaliji mentioned: As for oral medicine, it breaks his fast because something beneficial to the body reaches the interior, so it is like eating in meaning, but expiation is not obligatory because eating is absent in form. From Abu Yusuf, concerning snuff and oral medicine, there is expiation. If he takes snuff at night and it comes out during the day, it does not break his fast. The word 'medicine' was general, so it includes wet and dry because the criterion is reaching, not whether it is wet or dry. Al-Quduri conditioned wetness because wet usually reaches the interior, such that if one knows that wet did not reach, it does not break, and if one knows that dry reached, his fast is broken. Thus in al-'Inayah, but there remains the case where one does not know with certainty which it was—if it was wet, then according to Abu Hanifa, he breaks due to usual reaching; they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: No.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding what invalidates fasting and the rules of expiation. The author first discusses the generality of the ruling on expiation for intercourse during Ramadan: it applies to both genders, free and slave, and even if the sultan is the one fasting. He cites al-Bazzaziyyah and a fatwa by Abu Nasr Muhammad ibn Salam suggesting that for the wealthy, freeing a slave is recommended over fasting two months, though the purpose of deterrence remains. Then, he addresses the matn statement that no expiation is due for emission (inzal) that occurs without actual intercourse, i.e., through touching or contact short of the front or back passages. The fast is still invalidated because of the seminal emission, but since there was no intercourse in form, no expiation is required. This ruling is limited to interactions between men and women; the case of two women is mentioned as analogous. The author clarifies the linguistic meaning of 'farj' as the private parts in general, but legally both the front and back are considered private parts. Next, he comments on the matn that spoiling a fast other than Ramadan (like make-up or supererogatory fasts) does not require expiation. The reason is that the sanctity of Ramadan is unique; breaking it is a more severe offense. Therefore, other fasts cannot be analogized to it. The case of pilgrimage (hajj) is mentioned as an exception where spoiling a non-obligatory pilgrimage still requires a sacrifice (dam), but that is based on explicit text, not analogy. The main section deals with the entry of substances into the body through various means: enema, snuff, ear drops, and treatments for deep wounds or skull fractures. The hadith 'Breaking the fast is from what enters, not from what exits' is the primary proof, but it is restricted by the hadith on vomiting (which involves exit). The author explains that the fast is broken because something returns to the interior, even if minute. He also addresses an objection about menstruation and postpartum bleeding invalidating the fast despite being exits. The answer is that these invalidate due to legal incapacity, not by the general rule. He then delves into the pronunciation and definition of terms: 'ihtaqana' (enema), 'ista'ata' (snuff), 'aqtara' (drops), 'ja'ifah' (deep wound reaching the interior), and 'ammah' (wound reaching the brain). For the ear, he notes that oil is undisputed, but water is debated. Al-Hidayah holds it does not break the fast, while others say it does if poured intentionally. The relied-upon position from Fath al-Qadir is that if one pours water into the ear deliberately, it invalidates the fast because the water reaches the interior by one's own action. However, if it enters while bathing without intent, it does not break. The passage continues with various scenarios: inserting a food-filled string, thorough cleaning (istinja') that reaches the depth of an enema, inserting a dry stick or finger into the anus or vagina, being struck by an arrow or spear, and oral medicine (wajur). In all cases, the key principle is whether a substance that is beneficial to the body (like food or medicine) reaches the interior by one's own action. If it does, the fast is broken but without expiation, unless it was intercourse during Ramadan. Abu Yusuf held that snuff and oral medicine do require expiation, but the majority position is otherwise. The author concludes with a discussion of dry vs. wet substances: if the substance is wet, it is presumed to reach the interior, thus breaking the fast; if dry, it does not unless it is known to reach. At the end, he mentions a disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) when there is uncertainty about whether a wet substance reached. This passage demonstrates the Hanafi method of deriving rulings from textual proofs (the hadith) and applying principles of causation and customary practice.

Key Terms
المحقنة (al-mihqanah): An enema, i.e., injecting liquid into the rectum.
السعوط (al-sa'ut): Medicine that is snuffed or inhaled through the nose.
الجائفة (al-ja'ifah): A wound that reaches the interior of the body (jowf), such as a deep stab in the abdomen or chest.
الآمة (al-ammah): A wound that reaches the membrane surrounding the brain (umm al-dimagh), i.e., a deep skull fracture.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited in this passage, what is the basis for invalidation of the fast?
A) Exit of any substance from the body
B) Entry of any substance into the interior
C) Entry of a beneficial substance into the interior
D) Exit of a beneficial substance from the body

Key Takeaway
The fast is invalidated when a beneficial substance enters the interior of the body through any opening by one's own action, as established by the hadith 'al-fitr mimma dakhala', with the exception of invalidation due to legal incapacity (menstruation).
